If the moisture is beneath the bone, its sign is that you will see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water will sink when you press it with your hand; this should not be hidden from you. You should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner:.
After the incision, all the moisture should be drained out. Then, the incisions should be sutured and bandaged. For the first five days, they should be treated from above with poultices and oil. Afterward, the bindings are removed and the wound is treated with lint and ointments. Do not neglect to moderately bind the head, and nourish the patient with dry food that is low in moisture.
Until the organ is strengthened and healed, another method of incision is to look where the bone of the swelling and gathering of water appears because it may be more at the back of the head or in the front or on the right or on the left.
Aim the incision where the swelling and accumulation of water appear, make the incision as much as you can, but be careful not to cut an artery, causing bleeding that could lead to the patient's death along with the discharge of moisture.
The second chapter.
Cut the two arteries located behind the ears, which are known as the carotid arteries.
When one is afflicted with severe attacks to the eyes or the chest, and this persists without improvement from medication, the most effective treatment is to cut these two arteries.
If you intend to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a coarse cloth so that the artery becomes visible. Then secure the patientâ€™s neck with their garment. Look where the pulse beats; they are located in the two lower areas behind the ears, and they are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark them with ink and cut them with a sharp scalpel down to the bone. This should be done across the head. If you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery and lift it upwards by cutting. The length of the cut should be about two closed fingers.
If the pulse is cut, blood will gush out in a pulsating manner, spurting forward continuously. If the artery cannot be felt, you should measure about three finger-widths from the ear, mark with ink, and incise down to the bone. What should be done is to.
Six ounces of blood should be let moderately, and sometimes more or less may be let, all depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound; if there is any laudable pus remaining on the bone, cut it off.
To prevent a hot swelling, then bind the wound with a wick of linen cloth, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing the arteries located in the cheeks.
If a person suffers from chronic migraines or acute attacks caused by sharp humidities and heat in the cheek muscles, or severe chronic headaches and the like, and has been treated with various medical treatments to no avail, we have tried cauterizing or sealing the arteries in the cheeks for these diseases as described.
The method of cauterization involves shaving the hair from the cheeks of the patient and locating the visible artery in the cheek, as it can be identified by its pulse. It is rarely hidden except in a few individuals.
Or, in extreme cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with a fold of his garment, then rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel, which is depicted as follows. Then, gently cut the skin with it until you reach the artery. Then insert a forceps and pull it upwards until you extract it from the skin and free it from the surrounding adhesions on all sides. If the artery is thin, you can twist it with the tip of the forceps. Then a part of it is cut according to the distance between its ends and it contracts, and no bleeding occurs. For if it is not amputated and does not break, the blood will not clot at all. Then drain the blood from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong double thread, and let the thread be either from abriresm or from the tendons of the bow so that rot does not set in quickly before the wound heals and causes bleeding.
Then, cut the excess between the ties. Do this at that time or another later. If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a knife cauterizer until its ends are severed; it will perform the same function or even better as we have said.
Unless the patient has a fever or an inflamed temperament, for cauterization is one of the things that help in consuming moistures and thus is more beneficial. After extracting the arteries, the area should be filled with old cotton, and well-packed bandages should be placed on it. After removal, it should be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth and with bandaging until it heals, if God wills.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your work, immediately cut it either by cauterization or fill the area with pitch and hold tightly until the bleeding stops. If you do not have any of these, place your finger until the blood clots.
Cool the area with very cold water until the intensity subsides and secure it as needed. What is lighter and easier than extracting the artery is to cauterize the vein with this two-bladed cauterizer after marking the areas with ink.
And let it be very hot until it reaches the bone and sweat stops in two places so that the distance between its ends increases, for it will not heal with this cauterization at all. This is the image of the cautery iron.
The two knives are sharp, resembling small tips, except they are much less sharp than a knife because if they were as sharp as a knife, they would cool down faster and the flesh would not be cut quickly. If there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire will adhere to them.
And cut the flesh quickly, and this method is better than all others, being lighter and easier. Let the distance between the two knives be the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of persistent hot tearing to the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the outside of the skull, and if no treatment with medical drugs has been beneficial, and you see that the patient's face has reddened. And he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weak and moist, with his eyelids eroded and his eye sockets hollowed out by the intensity of the tears. Therefore, the first step in treating this condition is this method, which is called treatment with the sword, and it involves ordering the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then, make three parallel incisions on the forehead along its length. Each incision should be about two fingers in length. The first incision should be parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second slightly away from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on both sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws. Each incision should be about three fingers wide, and have with you pieces of prepared sponge or many dry cloths to stop the bleeding.
Then insert the sharp two-edged instrument which looks like this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, peel off all the skin and the fascia on the bone between the two incisions. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, then remove this instrument. You also insert another tool into the first incision, called a skeynai, which is sharp on one side and smooth and not sharp on the other side. This is its shape:.
The sharp side should be directed upwards toward the flesh attached to the skin, and the smooth side towards the bone. Push it until you reach the middle incision, cutting all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the outer surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other one. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the areas to remove clotted blood, then place a wick of cotton in each incision and put on it a dressing soaked in a drink and oil or vinegar and oil.
To prevent a hot swelling, on the third day, loosen the bandage and clean it thoroughly with lukewarm water. Then treat it with a salve of basilicon and rose oil, and other treatments used for wounds until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When a person experiences frequent, sharp, hot, and persistent discharges, and you see the eyes from it becoming weak and small, and their vision has weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and the eyelashes have fallen out. There will be a sharp, painful pain deep in the head and consecutive sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment for this is as follows: Order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the center of the forehead.
There will be a sharp, painful pain deep in the head and consecutive sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment for this is as follows: Order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the center of the forehead. Or raise it slightly with an incision, starting from the left temple to the right temple. The incision should go down to the bone, and keep your hand away from the temples' moving muscles when chewing. Once the bone is exposed, soak up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread, then secure it from above with bandages. Soak the bandages in a drink and oil so that no hot swelling occurs. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has broken down, you should scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophonium, preparing an ointment from them, and using it to promote flesh growth in such surgeries.
Chapter Six.
On treating what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is of four types: either a metal stone or semi-stone like iron and glass, or plant seeds like chickpeas and kernels, and the like, or a liquid such as water, vinegar, and the like.
Or an animal; if a stone or something like a stone that does not grow in the ear falls in, face the sun with the affected ear. If you see the stone, drip some violet or turpentine oil into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with cumin. And plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a band of cloth or wool around the ear and lifting the ear upwards. This treatment often causes it to come out. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, which is depicted in this manner.
If it comes out with the probe, and if not, try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved blind hook. If it does not come out that way, then make a tube of copper and insert one end of the tube into the ear hole.
And plug what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil so that there is no path for air other than the tube. Then draw it out strongly with your breath; this often causes it to come out. If it does not come out by the methods described, then take a small amount of the gum of the Anbat or the birdlime used to catch birds and place it at one end of the probe.
After applying tight cotton, then gently insert it into the ear hole after drying the ear from moisture, if it does not come out with all that we described, quickly proceed to incision before hot swelling or stiffness occurs.
The method of incision is to bleed the patient first and let blood out according to his strength. Then sit the patient in front of you, lift his ear up, and make a small incision at the base of the ear where the lobe is, at its lower part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can use. Then sew the incision immediately and treat it promptly until it heals.
And if what has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, try to remove it as mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a thin, delicate lancet shaped like this.
Attempt to cut that type of seed fallen into the ear with it. You should only do this if you are certain that the seed has been moistened by the steam in the ear until it becomes small pieces, then remove them with a blind forceps or a delicate spatula or by suction.
As mentioned, it is easy to remove them. As for water inside the ear, the patient should first try sneezing with cumin while filling his ear with some old cotton while lying down on the ear that contains the water, if it comes out that way.
Otherwise, take many thin and smooth stones along the fingers, warm them slightly by fire, then have the patient insert one of them into his ear hole, lie on one side, and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with a stone until all the water comes out, and the water may come out by taking from cotton or from feathers one, and insert one end into the ear and hold the other end in fire until most of it burns, then another feather is used.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as mentioned previously with the stone. As for extracting an animal that has entered, look to see if it is small in body like a flea and the like, then treat it as described in the division. And as for.
If its body is large and visible to the senses, try to remove it with pitch and tweezers, and its removal is easier than all that pierces the ear. As for removing the worm generated in the ear if you have treated it as mentioned in the section on fumigations and your treatment was ineffective.
You should look into the ear in the sunlight, and if any part of the worm is visible to you, remove it with pitch or with delicate tweezers. If nothing is visible to you, then take a tube, which looks like this.
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it. Do this repeatedly until all the worm comes out. If it does not come out, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you in the case of the stone.
If it does not come out with what we have mentioned, use the fumigations that I have described, which the ancients have tested for killing worms. You will find this in the section on fumigations. The application of ointments and medicines to the ear can be done with this device, whose shape is as follows:
As you see, it is made from silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you wish, the projectile inside the tube can be of well-made brass. If you prefer, take a rod and tightly wrap cotton around one end.
Then pour the oil or juice or any of these medications into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the rod with the cotton from the top and squeeze it moderately with your hand so that the oil is forced into the ear canal and felt.
Do this with the patient present, and let what you pour into the ear be warmed slightly over fire. Be careful that the thing which drips into it is not too cold or too hot, as the ear cannot tolerate such extremes.
Chapter Seven.
On the Treatment of Temporary Ear Obstruction.
Some children may be born with ear canals that are not perforated.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or the growth of flesh within it. This obstruction may be deep inside the ear hole, beyond the reach of sight.
It may also protrude slightly outside the hole and be visible. That which occurs deep in the ear and is not visible is usually difficult to treat, while that which is visible should have the patient's ear exposed to the sun. Look into it, and if you see the obstruction clearly, open it with a delicate surgical instrument, which would be of this form:.
Its tip should have a slight protrusion limited to some extent, while the rest of the scalpel is smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate forceps and cut it with extreme gentleness until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is in the depth of the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe and warm it slightly over fire. Then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient feels heaviness in their hearing, gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum, then insert a piece of cotton into the ear proportional to the size of the hole, and wrap it in Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are certain that the wound has healed, or take a piece of cotton, moisten it with water, and sprinkle it with powdered zinc.
Use it, and if you see the ear has swollen with a hot swelling, you should remove the cotton and replace it with another piece that has been wrapped in kerotie made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides. Then continue to treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear. Use the rest of the treatment that stops bleeding as mentioned in the section on styptics.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry. They should be grasped with a forceps or tweezers and cut out at their roots with a scalpel, then powdered zinc should be applied to the area.
If blood flows from it, cauterize it with a gentle lens-shaped cautery. Cauterize it better after the cutting. It often returns if any of its roots remain, and if you cauterize with fire, you burn those roots and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of transient cold in the eyelids.
Often, something resembling cold in its intensity and hardness appears in the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called cold, which is the congregation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment for this is to examine it; if the coldness protrudes on the outer side of the eyelid and can move in all directions, then its treatment is easy, so make aæ¨ªå‘åˆ‡å£ã€‚
Then, peel it gently from all sides until it is removed, then hang it with a hook and cut it off. If you cannot cut it without penetrating the eyelid, this will not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large,ç¼åˆå®ƒï¼Œå¹¶æ²»ç–—ç›´åˆ°ç—Šæ„ˆã€‚
If it is small, then there is no harm as the ointment will heal and bind it. If the coldness leans towards the inner side of the eyelid, turn the eyelid and hang the coldness with a hook without needing to make an incision, and cut it from all sides.
If you penetrate the eyelid with the cut, it will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the coldness with salt water and treat the area with a healing agent until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the sty that appears on the eyelid.
A sty is a pustule that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it most often affects children. It burdens their eyes and causes them to have discharges at night, and they are unable to look at sunlight. In order that the tears flow quickly from them.
Therefore, you will find them always leaning on their faces and sleeping that way, with their eyelids under the eyebrows being moist, swollen, and the sty clearly visible. If you press the area with your fingers, you will feel the sty between them. The procedure is for the patient to place his head in your lap.
Then take a piece of linen, prepare it into a wick, and make a circle from that wick around the sty on all sides. Then place it over it and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the sty in the center of the circle.
Then, with a sharp scalpel, make a transverse incision in the center of that moisture. The incision should not be larger than that made in a boil. As for depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the sty.
In most cases, the sty will protrude from the incision in the form of a white piece of pus. You should pull it out with a cloth wrapped between your fingers to the outside while rotating your hand right and left until it is completely removed. Be careful not to enlarge the incision so that the scalpel does not reach the eye and cause harm.
If the boil does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should increase the incision slightly gently until the boil appears, then pull it out as we said, then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and bind it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
He places it in the depth of the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing bandages until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the Types of Eyeshadow.
If excessive eyelashes grow outside the natural course beneath the natural eyelashes and persist, they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, drooping of the eyelids, whitening, and thickening until this becomes a cause for the failure of the eye.
Eyeshadow application is done in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by using a sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or by cutting and stitching, or by using reed, which I will mention.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, otherwise insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
You turn it with the spatula, then incise inside the eyelid without the excess hair from the larger opening to the smaller opening with a sharp scalpel. Then you insert the thread and place a small bandage of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of an oak leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to lift the eyelid, for this may vary among people, as some may need a suitable amount cut from the eyelid according to how droopy it is.
And some may need to cut less, all of this according to the looseness of the eyelid. Then, you incise with the scalpel along the two lines that were taught, starting from the larger opening to the smaller one, and each incision should be close to the natural hairline by about the thickness of a needle.
Then insert the forceps into one of the corners of the skin, then peel it all off. Then approximate the two edges with suturing using a needle and thin wool thread, wipe away the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substances.
If you wish to do that, and otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the sutures for about three or four days, then treat it. If you prefer, you can leave the wound unsutured and treat it with something that dries and constricts, as the eyelid will rise when the wound heals and comes together, but suturing is better.
This method of tightening was mentioned by the earlier authorities, except that it is laborious for the patient. It is a good work and there is no danger in it. Another method of tightening is to mark on the eyelid a shape like an oak leaf, as we described. Then lift the eyelid with three forceps, which can be spread out or grouped in this manner:.
Then cut the excess eyelid with a small scissors in this manner, cutting moderately:.
If you cannot control the tweezers and they do not suit you, take a needle with thread and insert it into the center of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger opening and a third thread near the smaller opening, then gather the threads between your fingers evenly, then lift them with your hand in an even lift.
Lift the entire marked skin as we described, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling appears when you cut or incise, soothe that swelling with kerotie and similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid turns inside out, and in such a case, you should use what we have mentioned of cutting, suturing, and cauterization with fire and sharp medicine. Cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, though cutting and suturing are more skillful in both cases.
Cauterization with reeds is done in this manner: you turn the eyelid inside out and make an incision on the inner side as described. Then, you make two thin reed or wooden strips, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you notch the ends of both on each side where the threads will hold. Then, gently bring them together over what remains of the eyelid and tightly secure the reeds on both sides. Leave it for days, as the tightened skin will die, turn black, and rot.
Until it falls off by itself. If it takes longer, use a rasp to notch it, then treat it until it heals. Once it has healed, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes will not turn inward towards the eye. This is the shape of the two reeds, made in this form and with the same length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On Removing Ingrown Eyelashes with a Needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and insert a thread of smooth fine silk into it, then gather its ends and tie them in an extremely gentle knot. The length of the thread should be about a span.
Then, attach another thin thread shorter than the first and tie its ends if you wish. The patient should place their head in your lap and it is best to be near sunlight so that the work can be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the loop into the root of the unwanted hair.
Pierce the eyelid quickly with it until you bring it out above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the loop and draw the hair into the loop if there is one or two or three hairs, not more.
Then pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the loop in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the loop and the hair does not come out, pull the loop downward with the thread that you attached to it until the loop comes out of the upper hole.
Then put the hair back in and pull it out. Do this until you see the hair rise in the upper hole with your naked eye. At that point, remove the loop and the thread, tighten the eyelid, and leave it tightened for two or three days until the hair stays in place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long natural hair to it so that it rises together, or leave it until it grows longer over time, and then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the treatment of sties that occur in the upper eyelid.
Which is affected by this sty is called arnabiyah, and this sty can either be natural or incidental. The incidental one results from the healing of a wound, split, burn, or the like.
The procedure is to incise that healed area, separate its edges, and place a wick of linen between them, securing it until it heals. It is not advisable to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
If you do that, the sty will return worse than before. Instead, use things that relax, such as fenugreek and lignum aloes soaked in water with alum and linen seeds, and a salve of diachylon dissolved with some oils, with which you anoint the wick and treat it.
The key to treating it is to ensure by every means that it does not heal in the same condition as before. The procedure we mentioned is part of correcting the sty, not returning the area entirely to its original state.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of sties that occur in the lower eyelid.
This sty that occurs on the lower part is what is truly called a sty. It can be natural or incidental. The incidental one results from a wound, split, burn, or the like.
The method for treating it is to take a needle with a doubled thread, pierce the flesh with it, and pass it from one side to the other until the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then stretch the flesh upward with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, well and good; otherwise, take a probe and place it on the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it, and make two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid so that the ends of these incisions start from both corners of the cut you made until they meet to form a corner. Until when they come together, its shape becomes similar to this shape:.
It is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh so that its sharp side is lower than what adjoins the eyelid, then join the separated parts with two stitches using wool thread, then treat it with the relaxants and lint we mentioned until it heals.
If the eyelid has become wide due to an incision, suturing, or cauterization, it should be cut with a simple incision without hair from the eyelashes as previously mentioned, then separate the two edges with lint as we described.
In summary, the treatment of ptosis, whether from above or below, should be carried out according to what appears suitable for the condition of the ptosis, as it may vary greatly in appearance, and the skilled practitioner will devise a method in whatever way is possible for him.
