Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people where the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from moving easily and hindering its natural functions; therefore, a thin spatula should be inserted under the eyelid.
And you raise it upwards or extend it with a forceps, then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel that is not as sharp as other scalpels but slightly blunt so as not to distress the patient during the procedure, which could cause the scalpel to slip and harm the eye with its sharpness. Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed. At that time, pour salty water into the eye to wash it, or drip a solution of zingiber officinale dissolved in water into it. Then, separate the eyelid from the eye with a cotton swab.
And place wool soaked in egg white over the eye, and after the third day, use moistened compresses until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the removal of the ingrown nail and excess flesh around it.
The ingrown nail can be of two types: either it is neural, resembling a thin, hard membrane, or it is non-neural, resembling a solid white moisture that breaks when touched by iron or when attempted to be removed with forceps.
And no forceps were used in it, and both types begin from the larger aperture until they are inserted little by little until they cover the eye and prevent light and movement of the eye, and the method of cutting them is that the patient puts his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the cataract with slightly curved forceps and pull it upwards, then take a needle, insert a hair from horse or ox hair and a strong thread into it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce it in the center of the cataract.
And you pass it through with the needle and tie the cataract with the thread, pull it upwards, and scrape with the hair along the side of the cataract that is next to the pupil as if you are spreading it out with the hair to its end, then cut what remains at the base of the aperture with a delicate scalpel or a small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the aperture.
So that cutting it does not cause a constant flow of tears, and the cataract can be pulled with forceps alone or with thread as we said, then scrape it with a smooth-edged scalpel which looks like this:.
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane so as not to cause a tear in it which would harm the pupil. Then after cutting, put some powdered salt or zingiber officinale in the eye and leave it for another day, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the cataract is not adherent and you cannot insert a needle into it or hold it with forceps, this type of cataract is often treated with medication as described in the classification, and it heals. If you want to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate, smooth, and weak-edged scalpel in this form:. Trim the nail from above gently with it. If you see that it becomes loose and crumbles due to the trimming, drip a little from your vial into the eye of Zingiber officinale or red zingiber officinale or powdered salt and bind the eye for another day.
Then repeat the procedure on it until it is all gone, unless you encounter a hot swelling in the eye; in that case, leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals. Then repeat the trimming procedure on it until it heals. As for the flesh growth in the eyelid.
If the growth severely hurts the eye, attach that growth to a hook and cut part of it, but do not overcut so as not to cause tearing. Then drip red zingiber officinale or zingiber officinale into the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the removal of sutures and any excess flesh that grows in the eye.
Red flesh may grow in the eyes of some people, layer upon layer, until it covers the pupil or approaches it or overflows onto the eyelids. Sometimes the eyelids may turn outward, and it resembles the rose of the jelenar.
The procedure for this is to have the patient place their head in your lap, then open their eyes, and use appropriate tweezers to remove all of that red flesh.
Or grasp it with a forceps or pincers then cut the flesh little by little until all of it is cut off, taking care of the eye so as not to harm it while working, and your cutting should be either with the scalpel which we described for cutting nails or with a small scissors. Like those with which you pick the threads, as its image will come in the next chapter following this chapter.
Once the cutting is complete and all the inflamed tissue has disappeared, fill the eye with ground salt or drip red sulfur into it along with similar caustic medicines. Apply cotton coated with egg white externally on the eye to prevent hot swelling.
If any inflamed tissue remains and the blood overpowers you and you fear hot swelling, then leave the eye alone and treat it with what soothes the swelling. Then return to it and continue the procedure until it heals. Do the same for any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this kind.
However, you should avoid treating any eye that is weak by nature or due to a condition with iron procedures. Do not involve yourself in any of these procedures until you see that the organ can withstand such treatment.
Chapter Eighteen.
On removing threads from the eye.
Threads are red veins that form over the eye, hindering vision and weakening the eye over time. You should first examine if the eye containing the threads is strong and does not have any other disease besides the threads.
Then at this point, use the ligation method, which involves instructing the patient to place his head in your lap, then suspend those veins with one or two forceps according to your skill, and the forceps should be gently curved in this manner:.
or they could be double forceps in one body as follows:ï¼Ž
Then, with a delicate scissors, gently grasp those veins and wipe the blood from time to time until you see that those veins have gone from the eye and the blood has clotted. Be careful not to harm the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done in the morning facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work so as not to cut anything other than those veins. When you are finished, drip green or red antimony into the eye to dissolve with its potency what remains of the passages. If you cannot extract it all at that time, bandage the eye with something that soothes the pain.
Leave it for days until the pain subsides and you are sure that the hot swelling is gone, then repeat the procedure in exactly the same manner until it heals. This is the image of the scissors used for this.
Leave it [with the patient's head in the lap of the student, another one drying the tears and examining the front and back of the eye closely]. The proper way to proceed is to open the eye with fixed or triangular speculums held by a student.
The patient's head should be in the lap of the student, another one drying the tears and examining the front and back of the eye closely. You may choose to start the ligation from the top of the eye or from the bottom, but it is preferable to use two forceps on the upper side, with one at the end of the iris and the other at the beginning.
Hold it between the middle finger and the index finger, then hang weights underneath opposite them. Then hold it between the little finger and the ring finger and lift your hand lightly until you see all the vein raised. Cut from the smaller side if you wish with scissors or with a sharp surgical knife.
You insert the needle with the thread and elevate the entire vein until it rises above the skin, and do not cut the tie because the fluid and blood will make it easier for you.
Then cut with scissors what exceeds the crown from above and below until nothing remains protruding except in the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea, peel it off with the needle; that is better. It may be possible in some people.
And when you cut it, the area will swell for days, the pain will subside, and the inflammation will decrease. Then repeat the procedure on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On Restoring the Nostril.
Physicians call the nostril a nares, and if they treat it with cauterization or with sharp, burning medicine as previously described and it does not heal, there is no alternative but to cut into the swelling when it has matured and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed, and if the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument that looks like this:
It is called the rough head, made from Indian iron, and its head is rounded like a button, engraved with a fine file or emery stone pattern. Place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly pressing your hand until you know that the corruption has been scraped off.
And do that repeatedly, then treat the area with drying and binding medicines. If the area is sealed and flesh grows there and the discharge stops, and it remains for forty days without recurring or causing any harm or incident.
Know that he is healed. Otherwise, there is no solution except to return the polyp to the nasal opening in this manner: reveal the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine, and when the bone is revealed, take it out in this form:
The tip of the iron should be triangular and its end conical, drawn to the tip as you see, then place it on the bone itself, and do this near the throat but keep your hand slightly away from the eye, then turn your hand with the instrument until the bone is penetrated and the patient feels it.
So that he feels the air coming out when he holds his hand over his nose. At this point, treat the area with binding and drying medicines as I have informed you. If the flesh grows and the place hardens, then the material which was projecting outward will return to the nose, making it easier on the patient, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty.
On the treatment of eye tumors.
If the entire eye is swollen and there is no harm or deficiency in sight, it is advisable to ease the patient, then fumigate the back of the head. Then place a leech on the occiput without any conditions and let it suck gently.
Then place on the eye a poultice made from colocynth, storax, frankincense, and asafetida. Then bind the eye tightly above the dressing with many compresses. The compresses are made by taking soft cloths, folding each one into four layers to make them thick.
Then place them one on top of the other according to what the eye needs, then bind them with a strong cloth tightly. Repeat the cupping by suction without restriction, then remove the cup and leave the eye bound for a day and night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened well. Then remove the bandage after a day and night as we said. If you see that the eye has returned to normal, otherwise repeat the dressing, padding, binding, and cupping until it recovers.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Cutting the Hernia.
If a hernia appears in the vaginal tunic and then swells and protrudes outside the eyelids like a grape, disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It involves inserting a needle into the base of the hernia from below to above, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread on the side of the opening and passing it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the part where the doubled thread bends, tie it around the base of the hernia with the needle, and tighten it well. Then remove the needle and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye, leave it until the threads fall off with the hernia, then treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, you may bind the hernia on all sides with a circle of cloth to increase the eye's protrusion. Then, use a thin scalpel that reaches the depth of the eye to let the egg-like moisture flow out, causing the eye to descend and wither in place, then bind it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of carbuncle.
This ailment which is called carbuncle is a kind of liquid that gathers in the eye, resembling flowing water but there is none. The method to treat this is to have the patient sit on a chair upright, then take hold of his head with both hands from both sides.
Move it until you see the liquid move to the bottom with your eye, then it stabilizes and does not move, and the light is released, and the patient sees things as he used to see them. If it does not move to the bottom, we know that it is water. If the liquid does not descend as mentioned,
otherwise, sit the patient in front of you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision above the cornea where the sclera connects to the cornea, until the material is released.
Once it is released, drip warm water mixed with honey or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled into the eye. Then continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which is suitable for suction with clear and explanatory words, so take that information as accurate. At this point, you should sit the patient in front of you, squatting opposite a light source near the sun.
You bind his healthy eye and tighten it well, then raise the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye that has the cataract is the left one or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the probe near the cornea with a thickness similar to that of a morshed in the white part of the eye from the side of the smaller opening, then push the probe strongly while turning it with your hand until it penetrates into the white part of the eye and you feel the probe reach an empty space.
It is necessary that the depth to which the probe goes should be equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the iris, for you will see the copper clearly through the transparency of the cornea, then direct the probe upwards to the place where the cataract is.
Then press it down repeatedly; if the cataract descends immediately, the patient will see what his sight opens up to him immediately with the probe still in his eye. Then let it rest for a while; if the cataract rises again, lower it once more without removing the probe.
When it has settled and does not rise, gently remove the probe while slightly twisting it with your hand little by little. Then dissolve some pure Andranian salt in water and wash the eye internally with it. Afterwards, apply moist cotton or wool externally to the entire eye.
With rose oil and egg white, bind it along with the healthy eye. As for us and people of our time, we apply ground cumin mixed with egg white on it. If the probe does not enter the eye due to its hardness, because there are those whose eyes are very hard indeed. It is necessary to take the surgical instrument which is called the picker, whose image is this:.
Pierce with it only the part that needs to be incised and do not deepen the puncture. It is merely to create a small opening for the probe, then insert the probe as mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare a firm bed for the patient where he can lie on his back in a dark room.
He should abstain from all movements and coughing, and his food should be what eases his digestion. He must not turn his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, then you remove it in that dark room and test his vision.
Show him things, then reapply the bandage until the seventh day. You should not do this during treatment or after cauterization at your time, but you should avoid it beforehand because water rises quickly with intense looking.
If a hot swelling occurs, you should uncover the eye before the seventh day and treat it with what calms the swelling. Once the swelling subsides, then release his sight and place a veil on his face through which he can gradually accustom his vision while he remains in that dark room for several days.
He should leave the house gradually and attend to his needs. Know that one who is learning cannot do without observing multiple times before proceeding with the work. I have heard from some Iraqis that they mentioned making a perforated probe in Iraq with which water can be suctioned.
I have not seen anyone in our country make this, nor have I read about it in the books of the early scholars. It is possible that this is a recent development. Here are images of various types of probes for those who may be unfamiliar with them:.
It is made of copper especially and its tip is shaped with this particular sharpness, triangular and acute.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of flesh grown in the nose.
Fleshy growths of various kinds may appear in the nose, some resembling many-legged scorpions, others being hard and cancerous with a dark color, and some being soft and not dark in color.
For those fleshy growths that are soft and not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit in front of you facing the sun. Open his nostril, place the forceps on those growths, then pull them outwards, and cut off what has been grasped with a delicate sharp scalpel from one side.
To ensure that all the flesh has been removed, if any part remains that you cannot cut, gently scrape it off with a delicate instrument until nothing remains. If bleeding overwhelms you or a hot swelling occurs.
Counter it with what is appropriate, or if it was a malignant swelling, act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is removed. Then, after cutting, apply vinegar and water or a drink in the nose. If the nose opens and moisture flows into the throat.
Know that it has healed if the moisture flows as it should. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that there is growing flesh inside on top of the loose bones which the instrument could not reach to cut. In this case, you should take a thread of coarse linen.
And make many knots in it, and leave the space of a finger or less between each knot. The patient should insert one end of the thread into his nose with a guide or as much as he can after making something like a button, and pull it until it reaches the nostrils.
It comes out at the throat, and boys often do such an action in school, which is easy for whoever wants to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, one end that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the thread with knots to cut the flesh; do this until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots. Then remove the thread, and in the nose, after wiping the blood, place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then the last thing to be placed in the nose is a lead tube for several days until it heals. If drying treatment is needed, use that method.
And this is an image of the device used to drip oils and medications into the nose:.
He places a small lamp made of silver or brass, with an open reservoir for oil and a passage for the wick.
And if you prefer, make the tube closed like a reed, and the oil reservoir of the device flat.
It has a handle at the end as you see, to hold when heating oil or any other liquid substances or liquids you desire.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing at the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow at the tip of the nose, increasing in size over time until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut off at the first sign of its appearance and entirely removed. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that can replace cauterization to the area. If it is not cut off until it grows large, examine it; if it is hard, solid, uniformly colored, and less sensitive.
Do not treat it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor; I have often seen that cutting this tumor results in great affliction for the patient. If the tumor feels soft to the touch and is not uniformly colored, and you see that it can be completely cut out. Purge the patient, and cut it out without hesitation. Treat the area with substances that dry and constrict until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If the connection is severed due to a wound or the like, know that if there is a separation in any of these cartilages, it seldom responds well to treatment except in some people.
When someone is presented with such a condition, if the wound is fresh and filled with blood, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the separation has caused the edges to part and each side has become whole, then you should incise each side to its thickness until it bleeds.
Then the lips are sewn together and tightened, and powdered saffron and frankincense are sprinkled over it. A plaster of unguent or another type of healing ointment is placed on top and left for two or three days, then it is removed.
And change the medicine until the threads fall off by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of sewing is to bring together what was separated either with needles as described in stitching the abdomen or with thread as previously explained.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots that appear in the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling morsels of corns and others smaller or larger. It is advisable to turn the lip, incise each knot, and grasp it with forceps.
Cut it from all sides, then fill the area after cutting with powdered zaj to stop the bleeding. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with binding agents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On removing excess flesh from the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called polypus. It is advisable to grasp it with forceps or hold it with a probe and cut it at its base. Allow the blood to flow out, then apply powdered zaj to the area.
Or one of the astringent drying agents, then if that flesh returns after treatment, it often returns; cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cleaning teeth with iron.
Rough and ugly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums. These scales may turn black, yellow, or green until they cause damage to the gums and spoil the appearance of the teeth.
It is necessary that the patient sit before you with his head in your lap, and you should clean the molar and tooth where the scales and sand-like substances appear until nothing remains. You should do the same for the black, green, yellow, and other discolorations until they are all cleaned.
If what is inside is removed on the first cleaning, otherwise repeat the cleaning on another day, a second and third time until you achieve your goal. Know that the molar requires various forms and many shapes of cleaning tools according to what is suitable for your work.
For the tool used to clean the molar from the inside is different from the one used to clean it from the outside, and the one used between the molars has a different form. Here are several forms of cleaning tools that you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On Extracting Teeth.
It is necessary to treat the molar for its pain with every means and hesitate to extract it, for there is no replacement if it is extracted, as it is a noble essence. Even if extraction is unavoidable.
It is necessary if the patient decides to extract it that you ensure the tooth causing the pain is accurately identified. For often the pain deceives the patient, and he thinks it is in the healthy tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted; we have seen barbers do this many times.
When you have accurately identified the painful tooth, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong instrument until the gum is separated from all sides, then move it slightly with your fingers or with delicate forceps little by little until it becomes loose.
Then take hold of it firmly with the larger forceps, and have the patient's head between your knees so that he is stabilized and does not move. Then pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments, insert it gently under the tooth from all sides, and rock it as you did before.
If the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill that hole with a cloth and secure it well with a thin strip of linen so that it does not crumble when you grip it with the forceps. You should also carefully incise around the gum on all sides.
Be cautious with your force so as not to break the tooth, leaving a piece behind, which would cause the patient greater harm than their initial pain. Beware of acting like ignorant barbers who recklessly extract teeth without using the methods we have described.
For often they inflict great calamities upon people, the least of which is that a tooth may be broken, leaving all or part of its root behind. Or it may pull out some of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times. Then, after its extraction, he rinses his mouth with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, which often happens, then crush some zadj and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, clean it if the zadj does not help you. The image of the delicate forceps that move the tooth first have long ends and a short handle to ensure they do not bend when you grip the tooth with them. This is the image of the larger forceps:
It should be as you see, with thick handles so that when you grip them, they do not give in or bend. The ends should be short and made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel with serrated edges at the tips that interlock to grasp firmly and tightly.
The ends may also be made like a file, which makes them strong and precise.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On Extracting Tooth Roots and Removing Broken Jawbones.
If after extracting a tooth, a broken root remains, it is advisable to place cotton with fat on the area for a day or two until the site relaxes, then you can insert the dental wax or forceps. which have ends resembling the mouth of a bird called the pelican. This is the image of the forceps:
It has been made like a scraper from the inside or like an extractor, then if it does not respond to you with these forceps, you should dig at the root and cut all the flesh with the lancet, then insert the tool that resembles a small lever, which looks like this:
Short at the end, slightly thick, and should not be hollow to avoid breaking. If the root comes out this way, otherwise, use these other tools whose shapes are as follows, the first is triangular at the end and somewhat thick:
An image of another delicate triangle:
We also use this tool with two prongs, which looks like this:
And with other tools and iron instruments that were mentioned earlier in the extraction of teeth. We also use this tool that resembles a large pair of forceps, which looks like this:
Triangular at the bent end, somewhat thick to avoid breaking, and should not be hollow. Know that tooth instruments are numerous, as well as other tools which are almost innumerable. The skilled craftsman may invent his own tools according to what work and diseases themselves suggest to him.
Because there are diseases for which the ancients did not describe tools due to the variety of their types. If a bone in the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth breaks or becomes rotten, examine it in its place with one of these instruments or forceps that were mentioned for extracting roots.
And we use this awl, which looks like this:
It should have some slight thickness to hold the bone so it does not slip until the bone comes out, and we set the area with suitable medicines. If there is a little putrefaction in the bone, remove its putrefaction and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the Extraction of Teeth that Have Grown in the Wrong Position.
If the teeth have grown out of their natural course, they spoil the appearance, especially if this happens in women and slaves. It should be examined whether a molar has grown behind another molar.
And if it cannot be extracted or pushed back, then extract it. If it is attached to another molar, cut it with this tool, which has this shape:.
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron with an extremely sharp tip. Your cutting of it should be done over many days due to the hardness of the molar so as not to disturb the other molars. If it protrudes firmly because of its coldness, heat it with a file that has this shape.
It should be made entirely of Indian steel, with a thin and delicate engraving like the file used for making needles. Use it to heat the molar gradually over many days gently so as not to shake the tooth loose and cause it to fall out. Finally, smooth it and remove it with some chisels.
If a part of the molar has broken and is hurting the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed down until the roughness of the break is removed, smoothed out, so that it does not hurt the tongue or interfere with speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On securing loose teeth with silver threads or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot chew any food without them falling out, and treatment with constricting medicines has not been effective.
The solution is to secure them with a gold or silver thread, and gold is better because silver can corrode and rot after a few days, whereas gold remains unchanged forever and does not suffer from that. The thread should be of moderate thickness to fit between the teeth.
The method of securing is to take the thread and insert its bend between two healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose tooth or teeth until you reach another healthy tooth on the other side.
Then, you return the weaving to the side where you started and pull gently and wisely so that it does not move at all. Secure the thread at the roots of the teeth so it does not come loose. Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them tightly, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not injured, then leave it secured in this manner as long as needed. If it loosens or breaks, secure it again with another thread. Thus, one can enjoy it for a lifetime. This is the image of the teeth and the method of securing two loose teeth between two healthy ones as you see.
And it may happen that one or two molars, after falling out, return to their place and fit as we have described and remain. This is only done by a skilled craftsman who can carve bone from the bones of cattle into the shape of a molar and place it in the spot where the molar has gone. And it is tightened as we said, so it remains and one benefits from it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue and hinders speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may either be natural, with which a person is born, or it may be accidental from a wound that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, placing their head in your lap, lifting the tongue, and then cutting that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is freed from its restraint. If there is some stiffness or knotting, and this is due to a healed wound, then make an incision with forceps and cut it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is resolved.
Be careful that the incision is not too deep in the flesh so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding. After the cutting, have the patient rinse with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a cotton wick under the tongue for the patient to keep each night.
To prevent it from healing again, if bleeding occurs, apply powdered zajac to the area. If the bleeding persists, cauterize the area with a suitable lens-shaped cautery tool. Then treat it with all treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On extracting the frog that develops under the tongue.
A swelling that resembles a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing it from its normal function, and it might grow until it fills the mouth. The procedure for this is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and examine the swelling.
If you see it is dark or black and hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not touch it for it is cancer. If it leans towards whiteness and has moisture, then puncture it with a probe and cut it open with a delicate knife and extract it from all sides.
If the blood overpowers you while working, apply powdered zaj to it until the bleeding stops. Then return to your work until you remove it entirely. Finally, rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with the appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the treatment of tonsil swelling.
And what grows in the throat from other swellings may present glands inside the throat that resemble those appearing outside, called tonsils. If treating them with what I have mentioned in the division does not heal them.
If the swelling is hard, dark in color, and has little sensation, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in color and its base is thick, do not treat it with iron either, for fear of bleeding. Instead, leave it until it ripens, and it will either dry up or burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, then this is what should be cut. The procedure involves checking before the operation whether its hot swelling has completely subsided or decreased somewhat.
Then sit the patient facing the sun, with his head in your lap, open his mouth, and take an assistant in front of you to press down his tongue with an instrument that looks like this.
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue with it and locate the swelling, once it comes into your sight, take a probe and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it out as much as possible without bringing any of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument that looks like this: it resembles scissors, but its ends are bent so that the tip of each is very sharp, facing the other. It is made of Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a lancet that looks like this:
It is sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. After cutting, the patient should gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or wormwood leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then continue to treat until healed. Sometimes other swellings may appear in the throat besides the tonsils; these should be cut in the same manner as described for cutting the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, almost reaching the larynx. It was slightly sensitive and was nearly blocking the throat; the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it prevented her from eating or drinking water.
She was on the verge of death had she remained another day or two, and the tumor had branches that extended out of her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserting a clamp into one of them and pulling, thus removing a piece successfully.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril opening, and did the same with the part that emerged from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pushed her tongue aside, inserted the clamp into the tumor itself, and cut a portion of it, from which only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water and then eat. We repeatedly cut the tumor over a long period of time, and the tumor would regenerate what was removed until that process extended for a long time for both of us.
I resorted to cauterizing the tumor inside her throat, which stopped its growth. Then I left that place and did not know what happened to her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On Cutting the Tumor of the Tonsil.
What is called a uvula, if a phlegm descends to the tonsil and it swells, and if it is white and elongated, then it is called a pillar. If it is thick at the bottom and round, then it is called a uvula. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment does not work.
I saw the hot swelling had subsided from it and it was thin, so it should be cut. And whatever of it was gathered round, circular, and did not have length, and was blood-colored or purple or black or without feeling, then it should be avoided from being cut.
In it, you should operate on the patient; therefore, if you see it as described in its whiteness and length, sit the patient facing the sun, press his tongue with the tool that was previously described, then pierce the reed into the growth.
And pull it down and cut it with one of the two tools I mentioned for cutting the tonsils. It is advisable to only cut what has exceeded the natural state without excess, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting, use what we described for cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is hesitant about the cutting, you should employ a cautery without fear or hesitation. The cautery here is done with a sharp remedy, placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then press his tongue with the tool we mentioned, then take some sharp water as described in the section on cauterization, and mix it with unslaked lime, neither too thick nor too thin. Fill this mixture into the hollow of this tool, and here is its shape:.
The end where you place the medicine should have a depression like that of a spoon. Place the tool with the medicine directly on the ulcer, with the patient lying on their side so that saliva flows out of his mouth from inside his throat, so that nothing goes down into his throat and causes harm.
Then hold the medicine in your hand and squeeze it on the tooth for about half an hour until you see that it has turned black and the pain of the medicine's bite has subsided, and if you want to take cotton and wrap it around the tip of a stick and moisten the cotton with the medicine.
You insert the stick with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton sticks to the tooth. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired effect on the abscess, then leave it; it will wilt and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After cauterization, wipe around the abscess with cotton soaked in fat and dry the area around it from the medicine. Then rinse with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and more reassuring.
Teeth can also be treated with a milder method than cauterization and cutting; they can be treated with fumigation as follows: take frankincense, saffron, thyme, spikenard, sage, chamomile, rue, and similar herbs. Gather all of them or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil the pot, which is sealed with clay from a wise person, and make a hole in the center of the lid. Attach a hollow instrument to this hole, made as follows: it can be made of silver or brass.
Insert the end with the funnel into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the tooth through the tube until the abscess swells well. Then repeat this several times until it wilts. Do not perform this treatment at the first sign of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling, and it should only be done when the hot swelling has subsided. If this tool is not available to you, take a reed and attach a chicken eggshell to one end so that the mouth of the patient does not get burned.
For the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is good for treatment with safety.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On removing thorns and other objects that pierce the throat.
Often, a bone or fish spine or something similar pierces the throat. You should extract what is visible to the eye after pressing the tongue with an instrument toward the sun.
So that you can see what is in the throat. For what does not appear and is hidden in the throat, you should induce vomiting before the food is digested in his stomach, as the piercing object may come out with the vomit, or you may have the patient swallow a piece of lettuce or the root of chicory.
Or have the patient swallow a bite of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, then have the patient swallow it. When it reaches the location of the spine, pull the string quickly. Do this several times.
For the spine or bone often gets stuck in it and comes out. If it does not come out as described, then use a lead instrument shaped like this:.
It should be slightly thicker than a probe and have a curved end. The patient should gently insert it into his throat while raising his head up, being careful not to touch the larynx to avoid causing a cough, using it to push out the bone or spine, or the doctor may insert it with their hand.
And inserting the affected area into it is better for knowing the position of the embedded object and pushing it downwards or pulling his hand up with the tool according to what seems appropriate until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On Extracting Embedded Thorns in the Throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the division regarding treatment with medications and it does not work, then examine the patient's throat in the sunlight after pressing his tongue with the tool that was described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a fine, firm tweezers. If you cannot do so, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then introduce an iron rod heated by fire into the tube.
Do this several times and the patient should abstain from water for the entire day. Then, take a vessel filled with cold water, open his mouth over it, and have him rinse with it without swallowing a drop. He should move the water around periodically with his hand.
The leech will fall off when it senses the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and alum using the tool described for fumigating the lungs. Do this several times, and it will fall off.
The method of fumigation involves taking a pot with hot charcoal, covered with a lid that has a hole in the middle. Attach one end of the tool to this hole, then place the fumigants inside. The patient should put his mouth on the other end of the tube and seal it tightly so that no smoke escapes until he feels the fumes reach his throat.
For the leech falls off at its place, and if it does not fall off, then the incense is repeated several times, and the patient endures thirst and eats salty food and garlic and does not drink water, so it must come out with this management. And this is a picture of an instrument that attracts the leech from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles forceps as you see, except that it has this bent part which enters the throat and its ends resemble a bird's beak with the roughness of a file so that when it grips something, it does not leave it.
And another instrument used to press the tongue, it is a wide iron ring the size of what fits inside the mouth above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so the mouth remains open against the will of the patient and he cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth to this same measurement.
Chapter Forty.
In it, there is discussion about compressing tumors and incising them.
Tumors have many diverse types, as will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in compression and incision in two aspects: one is the type of the tumor itself and what it contains of moistures.
The second aspect is due to the locations where they occur in the body, because a tumor that occurs in the head is different from a tumor that occurs in the thigh, and a tumor that occurs in a fleshy area is different from a tumor that occurs in a joint. Each has its own specific treatment method.
There are swellings that should not be lanced until the pus within them is ripe and complete, and there are those that should be lanced while they are still immature and have not fully ripened, such as swellings that occur near joints.
For if a swelling occurs near a joint and persists until the surrounding area becomes putrefied, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing disability of that limb, or the swelling may occur near a vital organ.
For if you delay lancing it until it ripens, you will harm the vital organ, or if it is near the rectum, lance it while it is still immature. It is necessary to lance a swelling that has not fully ripened when it is near the rectum so that the pus does not spread inside the rectum and become an abscess.
And it becomes such that it does not heal. You should know the time to lance swellings that have fully ripened, which is when the pain of the swelling subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and inflammation decrease, and the head of the swelling becomes defined, along with other signs.
You should place the incision at the lowest part of the swelling if possible, so that it is easier for the pus to drain downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the swelling. The incision should be made longitudinally along the body if the swellings are on limbs such as the hands and legs.
Places where muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries are located, generally in all flat areas without bends. As for those that bend, the incision should be made according to the nature of that area. If the swelling is in fleshy areas, it is best to refrain from making an incision.
Until it fully matures as we said completely, for if you sutured it before that, the pus would continue to flow from it for a long time and would be abundant in harm and filth, and its lips might become hardened and concave. Some swellings may spread across the body when necessary or according to what the limb requires.
It is appropriate to use one suturing for small swellings and a wide suture or many incisions according to the size of the swelling for large ones. There may be some swellings where it is necessary to pierce the skin and cut if it has become like a rag.
It has happened to some extent that it dies, similar to what occurs in many abscesses and hidden sores. Some are cut with a three-cornered incision, and some are cut in the shape of a plant leaf, like Arabian swelling.
Some require a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar types of incisions. For those that do not have a head, such as flat, smooth swellings, it is appropriate to use only a simple suture. If the swelling is large and has gathered for a long time, after suturing it, you should not rush to extract all the pus at once.
Rather, extract some of it, then leave the swelling for another day, and then extract some more pus. Do this gradually several times until all of it is extracted.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden release of pus, and the patient may die without you realizing it. Beware of this matter greatly.
After you incise these tumors, it is necessary to clean the wound and examine it. If the tumor's cut is small or if there is only one simple incision, then use linen or old cotton for bandaging. If the tumor is large and there are many incisions from the incising.
It is necessary to insert a wick into each incision until they all connect with each other. If some of the skin has been cut from the tumor or if it has been hollowed out, then it should be filled with old cotton or linen down without moisture and kept tight until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of linen in them and apply it to the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding persists, it is necessary to use the procedures, treatments, and evacuations that we have described in many places in this book and from the division. If you are in winter and the site of the tumor has many nerves, then soak the bandages with a hot drink and oil.
Place them on the area. If you are in summer and the areas are fleshy, then the bandages should be soaked with cold water and oil or a cold drink and oil. On the third day, as we said, it is necessary to cleanse the tumor and wipe it.
Use similar treatments for its cure until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the details, I have described each tumor and the way to treat it in a summarized and categorized manner.
Chapter Forty-One.
On Incising Tumors That Appear on the Scalp Skin.
Tumors appear on the scalp skin, and they are a type of swelling contained in membranes that have sacs like those of a chicken's egg. There are many types; some are fatty, and others contain moisture similar to mud.
Some contain moisture resembling pus and broth, and so on. Some are hard and solid. All of them can be safely incised and removed unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument that will be depicted after this, called a lancet, to determine their contents. If they contain moisture, then incise them longitudinally with a simple cut as follows:
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. Once the moisture has drained, peel off and completely cut out the sac that contained the moisture, leaving nothing of it behind, as it often returns if any part remains.
Then, soak a cotton pad in the pure ointment if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it for another day as it will eliminate any remaining parts of the sac. Then, reapply the cotton pad with the ointment again and possibly a third time if needed.
Continue until you are certain that no part of the sac remains. At that point, treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains fatty matter, incise it in this manner:.
Make a stiff incision and place the forceps on both sides of the wound, peel it from all sides, and try your best to extract the peritoneum that contains it. If an artery is encountered, do as described for you afterward, and similarly, if there is a hard tumor, make the incision accordingly.
The treatment is the same as mentioned, and incising the hard tumor is easier because it has less blood and moisture. Some of these tumors in the head in some people have no moisture at all. This is because I made an incision on a tumor that was.
I made an incision on a tumor on an old woman's head and found the tumor to be like a hard, rough, white stone that could not be broken; if thrown, it would injure someone. None of the other tumors occurring in the head were like this, such as the tumors that appear on children's heads.
At the base of the ears, make a slight incision all around and ensure its inner part is directed downwards to facilitate the flow of material downward. Then treat it with the appropriate treatment.
The Forty-Second Chapter.
On Incising Swellings in the Neck: These tumors often appear in the neck, under the armpits, and on the shoulders, and may occur throughout the body. The ones that appear in the neck can be singular or multiple, and some may develop from others.
Each of these tumors is enclosed in its own peritoneum, similar to boils and head tumors as described. There are various types of these tumors; some are hard, some contain moisture, and others are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
What I have seen of it was in good condition to touch, its appearance was close to the color of the skin, it moved in every direction, and it was not attached to the neck's nerve or windpipe or artery, nor was it deep-set. It should be cut with a simple incision from top to bottom of the body in this manner, from line ~G~ to line ~B~:.
And peel it from all sides and stretch the edges of the skin with forceps or with two forceps or with many forceps if you need to, as we said in the case of head tumors. Extract it little by little, and make sure on the neck not to cut a vein or nerve. The scalpel should not be very sharp.
So that your hand does not overcut or disturb the patient, causing you to cut what does not need to be cut. If you cut a vein or artery and it hinders your work, put in the wound powdered zagger or some hemostatic agents that stop the blood.
And suture the wound, leave it until the severity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to show signs of suppuration, for the blood will stop then. Return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if any more small growths remain, cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the growth, you should not cut it from its root but should tie it with a doubled tight thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm. Then fill the wound with old cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment and treat it.
And if the entire boar's [tusk] is cut, it is necessary to bring together the edges of the wound and suture it immediately after you ascertain that no fragment remains. If you find that a fragment of the boar's bone remains.
It is advisable to aim for cutting at its root and base, and to use suturing as we have mentioned. For those wounds caused by pigs that contain moistures, lightly bandage where you see signs of healing, and make the bandage towards the lower part as we said.
Then after the bandaging, use winding with the Egyptian ointment or the like to eliminate any remaining corruption. When you see that the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On incising the throat for a swelling that occurs inside the neck.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it, and this is the pattern of their speech. As for those with goiter, they should avoid incising the throat as they do not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and lungs are diseased, as for those who have a hot swelling in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no issue with the trachea, it is necessary to use incising the throat to avoid the damage caused by suffocation.
If we intend to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the trachea with a small transverse cut between two rings, similar to the incision in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage, and this position is appropriate for the incision.
For it is lacking in flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the practitioner is timid, it is necessary to stretch the skin of the throat with a speculum then cut the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then cut the peritoneum that we described, and identify the incision of the trachea from the phlegm that comes out with it along with the coughing, and the cessation of sound. Leave the wound open for a while; when the time of fear of suffocation has passed, bring together the edges of the wound from the skin.
And outline it alone without the cartilage, then use medications that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book says, the explanation of this statement we have quoted is that if they see the patient has had his throat blocked by one of these tumors they mentioned.
When the patient is on the verge of death and his breath is about to cease, they go to incise the larynx so that the patient can breathe through the wound, thus saving him from death. They ordered leaving the wound open until the period of illness passes, which usually lasts for three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it heals. What I have witnessed myself is that a servant took a knife and directed it at her throat, cutting part of the trachea. I was called to treat her and found her gasping like a slaughtered animal, so I exposed the wound.
I found that the blood coming out of the wound was minimal, so I was certain that no artery or vein had been cut. The air was escaping from the wound, so I immediately outlined the wound and treated it until it healed. The servant did not suffer anything except a hoarseness in the voice, nothing more.
And after days, it returned to its best condition, so from this we say that splitting the larynx has no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On incising the tumor that appears in the throat from the outside, which is called pharyngeal polyp.
This tumor, which is called pharyngeal polyp, is a large tumor of the body's color and it is common in women. It comes in two types.
Either it is natural or incidental. As for the natural one, there is no remedy for it. As for the incidental one, it comes in two forms: one similar to fatty tumors, and the other similar to the tumor that results from the entanglement of arteries, and incising it is dangerous. It should not be treated with iron at all, except for the small ones. If you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not attached to any vessels, then incise it as you would a fatty tumor. Chapter Forty-Five.
On Incising Various Types of Goods.
The types of goods are numerous, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a commodity and an excise, as it is confusing. I say that the excise is accompanied by heat and fever and alarming pains until the boiling of the excess subsides. And the rot completes, then the fever and intensity subside. The commodity is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pains; it is contained in a serous sack which is its special container, and it takes on the color of the body. Its beginning is like a lentil and it becomes like a bean, larger or smaller.
It exists in two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are numerous, as I have mentioned in the classification. When you proceed to treat the commodity, you should first probe and examine it with an instrument called the medsa, whose image will be shown in the section that follows this one.
Measure the size of the swelling, then remove the medsa and look at what comes out in its trace. If a flowing moisture of any color comes out, incise it slightly as I mentioned for other swellings. If no moisture comes out after the medsa, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have informed you, and suspend it with forceps. Gently peel the skin from all sides and be cautious with the sack so that you can remove it intact along with the commodity if possible.
If the cyst bursts during the operation and you cannot extract it intact, this often happens; then extract it piece by piece until nothing of it remains, for if any part of it remains, whether little or much, the disease will return most of the time.
If it overpowers you and a small amount remains, then widen the wound at your completion with some sharp, corrosive agents, suture the wound, and apply something that soothes the inflammatory heat on top of it. Treat it with all treatments until it heals. If the disease is large, incise its edges and treat it with what causes healing.
If you encounter a vein, whether it is prominent or not, and bleeding occurs, then immediately widen the area with powdered zadj and leave it tied for two or three days until the wound becomes septic and the blood's inflammation subsides. Then return to cutting what remains of the disease.

Chapter Forty-Nine.
On incising the tumor that appears due to an artery or vein, which is called an aneurysm.
If an artery is cut and the skin above it heals, a tumor often results from this. Similarly, a vein may also swell and become tumors.
The signs by which it is known whether the swelling and inflation are from an artery or from a vein: If the swelling is from an artery, it will be elongated and gathered in the depth of the body; and if you press the swelling with your finger, you will feel it as if it has a rustling sound.
The swelling that is from a vein will be round on the surface of the body, and incising these swellings is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body where they are very severe; therefore, their treatment with iron should be avoided.
And those that are also in the limbs or head should be avoided. As for those that are from the artery's mouth swelling, make a longitudinal incision on the skin over it, then open the incision with forceps, then peel and free the artery from the adhesions.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle under it and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places as I have explained for you in ligating the arteries in the cheeks. Then, incise with a scalpel the area between the ties until all the blood within it is released.
The swelling will subside, then use treatment that promotes pus formation until the ties fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is from incising a vein, you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as you can with your hand, then insert a needle below the area you are holding and pass it through with a doubled thread until it emerges on the other side. Then bind the tumor tightly with a knot as I have described to you in binding the hernia from both sides with the ends of the thread, and if you fear that the threads may slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
Tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its center until what is inside comes out. Then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied. Finally, place a bandage that has been soaked in a decoction and oil on it. Then use the treatment which involves bandaging and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that results from nerve torsion.
Just as a tumor may occur in an artery or vein, so can a tumor occur in a nerve if it is struck or due to excessive fatigue or the like. This is most common in the areas of the wrists and ankles.
And in every place where joints move, it is a hard tumor that resembles the color of the rest of the body and is mostly painless. If pressed firmly, the patient feels a slight numbness, and the tumor is not deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and can move in every direction without going forward or backward. Those that occur in the joints should not be treated with instruments, as they may cause paralysis. Those that occur on the head or forehead are different.
Incise the skin with a lancet. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it out from the root. If it is large, attach it with a hook, peel it off, then remove it. Finally, close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.

Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the Treatment of Umbilical Hernia.
Umbilical hernia can result from various causes, either from a split in the abdominal lining through which the intestines protrude, similar to other hernias, or from blood that emerges from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or from air that becomes congested there.
If it is due to a split in the abdominal lining and the protrusion of the intestines, then the color of the swelling will be similar to the color of the body, it will be soft without pain, and it will appear different in various positions. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestines, its appearance will vary more than what we have described. When you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns. It may also be accompanied by rumbling, and it often enlarges when entering a bath or experiencing severe fatigue. If it is due to moisture, then it will be soft.
It will not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor will it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then in addition to these signs, the swelling will appear blackish. If it is due to growing flesh, the swelling will be protruding and hard, remaining of a fixed size.
If it is due to air, touching it will be soft. In such cases, you should refrain from treating it, as there is fear and risk involved, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned tumors that occur due to arteries or veins.
If the navel protrudes due to the intestine or feces, it is necessary to instruct the patient to hold himself and stand upright extended, then mark with ink around the entire navel. Then, instruct him to lie down in front of you on his back, then incise with a wide scalpel around the navel at the place marked with ink, then pull the center of the swelling upwards with a large forceps, then tie the incised area tightly with a strong thread or silk.
The knot of the ligature should be a noose, then open the center of the pulled swelling above the ligature and insert your index finger and seek the intestine. If you find that it has been taken by the ligature, loosen the noose and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find feces, pull it out with forceps.
And cut its excess, if an artery or vein obstructs you, tie it well and return to your work. Take two needles, put strong threads through them, and insert the two needles into the incision you made around the swelling, piercing through, then pull the swelling tightly at four points with the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the pulled flesh rots and falls off by itself or cut it when it becomes thin and rotten. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the protrusion of the navel is due to grown flesh.
Or if it is due to moisture, you should incise the swelling as mentioned and remove the blood or moisture found within. Then treat it with something that closes the wound.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the Treatment of Cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medicines and warned against treating it with iron so that it does not become ulcerated, and we have mentioned the cancer that develops in the uterus and warned against treating it.
The ancients have mentioned that when cancer is in a place where it can be entirely excised, such as cancer in the breast or in the thigh and similar organs capable of complete removal, especially if it is newly arisen and small. However, if it has advanced and become large, it should not be approached for treatment.
Indeed, I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that. If it can be treated as we said, you should proceed by purging the patient with black bile several times, then purge it if it is in the veins and there is a clear fullness.
Then, you should place the patient in a position suitable for the procedure. Then apply the appropriate cauterization to the cancer and burn it from all sides along with the skin thoroughly until none of its roots remain, allowing the blood to flow without quickly stopping it.
Rather, squeeze the areas and completely drain all thick blood with your hand or with whatever tools you have. If a significant bleeding obstructs you during the procedure due to cutting an artery or vein, cauterize the vessel until the bleeding stops, then continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.

Chapter Fifty-Seven.
On the purification of children and the treatment of errors that affect them.
Circumcision is nothing more than the separation of a connection, like other surgeries. However, since it is performed by our will and is used specifically for children, it is necessary to follow the best method of procedure.
The easiest path leading to safety, so I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their religious laws; rather, it is something we have acquired.
Through experience, I found that the majority of craftsmen and cuppers use purification with a cautery and scissors, and they use the falkah and binding with thread and cutting with the nail, and I have tried all these methods.
I have not found anything better than purification with scissors and binding with thread, because purification with a cautery often adheres to the skin; since the skin of the covering has two layers, it may cut the upper layer while leaving the lower layer intact.
He is forced to make another cut and renew the pain. Purification with the falkah does not prevent cutting the tip of the urethra, as it might enter its hole. As for purification with the nail, the skin may tear, spoiling your work, or the boy's skin might be naturally short.
Many are born this way and do not need purification, and I have seen that. As for purification with scissors and binding with thread, experience has shown me its advantage because the scissors make appropriate cuts, as the blade on top is like the blade on the bottom.
When you squeeze your hand to align the blades, you cut uniformly and simultaneously, making the thread ligature resemble a wall around the urethral skin on all sides, thus avoiding any error. The first step of the procedure is to prepare the boy.

Then clean it from what dirt accumulates in it, then tie the marked area with a doubled thread, then tie a second knot slightly below it. Chapter Fifty-Eight.
On the treatment of retained urine in the bladder.
Retained urine in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, congealed blood, pus, or protruding flesh, and the like. If you treat it with the various treatments described in the section mentioned.

It is made of silver and should be thin, smooth, hollow like the quill of a bird's feather, about a span and a half in length, with a gentle spout at its end. The way to use it for drawing urine is to take a bent thread and tie wool or cotton tightly at one end.
And you insert the end of the thread into the bottom of the catheter and cut with pliers if any part of the wool remains so that it enters the tube and plugs it like a cork, then you grease the catheter with oil or butter or egg white and seat the patient on a chair.

It is made in this manner because the passage through which urine flows has a bend. Then, pull the thread with the wool tightly a little, for the urine will follow the wool, then remove it and the urine will flow out, and you can reuse the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
