It should not be untied as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not fall off until it heals. Lime alone can do this if frankincense and myrrh are unavailable.
This action can also be done with alum in many large wounds if its placement and binding are skillful, and the wounds are fresh with blood, provided that the air has not altered them. However, if the air has somewhat altered them, especially in summer time.
There is no effect in applying this powder; treat it as mentioned by applying some ointments that ripen it, or apply a porridge made from barley flour with water and honey until the pus discharges, then continue treating it with other treatments until it heals.
If a fracture occurs in the bone along with the wound and it is minor, treat it with a splint. I have described the treatment of skull fractures in what follows, in neck surgery. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between a simple neck wound and a head wound.
If a nerve or an artery in the neck has been cut, and if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with strong constrictors like zincagar and zaaj, as these can harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
Nothing is to be placed on the wound that is cold at all because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs, exists. What you treat it with of soft medicines should be like washed cotton cleansed with fresh water several times, moistened with oil or rose fat and tutia.
If it is also washed with fresh water and all the metallic stones of this description and moist ointments, and if the wound is large, use suturing or bring its edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and pus has gathered in a hidden place at its bottom, then line it with lint in the lowest part. If an artery is cut off in the wound and you see blood flowing, then cut it or tie it or cauterize it if the situation necessitates that.
If the wound has cut some or all of the laryngeal rings but the vocal cords are intact, then bring the edges of the skin together with sutures around a tube in the larynx. Do not touch the larynx itself; rather, set it and return it to its natural shape, then secure it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it closely; if there are fragments of bone in it, remove them all or as much as possible, and leave what you cannot remove until the wound becomes septic and heals, making their extraction easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we described. If bone becomes septic in the wound and turns into a sinus, treat it as will be mentioned in its section, in the surgery of the chest and what is between the shoulders.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see it has depth, then look; if air comes out when the patient breathes, know that it is a fatal wound. If it does not have depth and is soft with blood, do not apply cautery to it at first, nor bind it tightly lest the blood be retained in its depth.
It leads to the heart and kills the patient, but apply an attractive poultice to it and change it twice a day. If you do not have a poultice, put old cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb what comes out of it in terms of moisture and let the patient sleep on the side of the wound.
Let what accumulates in it flow out. If three days or more have passed since the wound occurred, and the patient has not experienced convulsions, nor bad palpitations, nor shortness of breath, and you see that all his conditions are good, then know that the wound is sound. Treat it with lint and other treatments until it heals.
If the pus cannot be seen and the discharge continues constantly, then know that it has become a fistula. Treat it from its opening. Let the patient lie on the side of the wound so that what accumulates in it flows out. If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it with the previously mentioned suturing method.
If it is large, treat it as such, or use cautery if it is small. If it has affected the bone and broken off shards, examine the wound and promptly remove those shards if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until they are.
Let the wound become putrefied, for it will make the shards easier to remove. As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment follows the same principles as what has been mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On abdominal surgery, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound that affects the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. The large one may cause the intestine or several intestines to protrude, making their reinsertion and suturing more severe and difficult.
The small wound may also be difficult in another way, for its closure becomes more challenging. If you do not promptly return the intestine to its place, it will swell and thicken, making reinsertion difficult. Therefore, the medium-sized wound is considered better because returning the intestine is not as difficult as it is with these two types.
Know that suturing the abdomen is done in four ways. Two of these methods are general and suitable for suturing the abdomen as well as other body wounds. The other two methods are specific and apply to all wounds, but they are particularly specialized for suturing the abdomen.
I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and detail. So, I say that if the wound is small and part of the intestine protrudes from it and cannot be returned easily, this is due to one of two reasons: either because the wound is small as we have mentioned, or because the intestine has become swollen due to the cold air.
If this is the case, it should be warmed by soaking a sponge or a wet cloth in lukewarm water alone or in which aqil, sa'di, and sunbul have been boiled, and apply it to the intestine until the swelling subsides.
And it can be done with a drink that has binding properties, and it is better than water alone in dissolving the inflation. So if you see that the inflation has dissolved, then moisten the area with water in which alum or baker's soda has been boiled, for it will facilitate its entry with less effort.
If it is not possible for it to return after this treatment, then slightly cut the wound with the instrument used for cutting abscesses. The first treatment, if effective, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to out of necessity and when other methods fail. This is the image of the instrument:
One of its curved sides should be limited while the other side is unlimited. The thin end should not be as thin as a scalpel but slightly blunt. It resembles a small spatula as you see. If the wound widens and the intestine enters, it should be returned to its natural position.
And if you can place it in its specific position, that is best. However, if the wound is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head. If it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
And if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to have the side with the surgery higher than the other side. This should be applied in major surgeries as well as moderate ones.
As for small surgeries, place it according to what is feasible for you. Then have a gentle assistant ready who holds the entire wound with both hands, brings its edges together, and gradually exposes it bit by bit to the person suturing it.
And this is the description of a common suture, where you take one needle or several needles according to the width of the wound. Then, leave from the edge of the tear an amount equal to the thickness of the little finger and pierce with one needle without inserting thread into both edges of the skin. And with both edges of the peritoneum under the skin from inside until it comes out from that side, having gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum making four layers, then tie with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound come together firmly.
Then leave an amount equal to the thickness of a finger and pierce another needle, then interlock it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with however many needles are needed until the entire wound is sutured. The needles should be of medium thickness between thick and thin.
For needles that are too thin easily cut the flesh, and those that are too thick have difficulty entering the skin. Therefore, they should be of medium thickness between thin and thick. The needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too closely.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not open before healing, nor should it be too far apart in suturing so that the wound is prevented from closing. Then cut the ends of the needles so they do not harm the patient while sleeping, and make ties from linen cloth on each side to secure the ends of the needles.
Leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is most suitable for small surgeries, as they may only require one or two needles or so.
As for the method of suturing in general surgery, it is to bring together with a suture the four edges, namely the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum at once with a needle that has twisted thread moderate in thinness and thickness. Then, when this needle has penetrated the four edges, return the needle from the side where you started.
Let the thread be intertwined from the top of the wound so that the suturing is according to the way a sack is sewn to pack belongings, and there should be between each suture the thickness of a little finger, and this kind of suturing is known by all people.
With this suturing, a surgery was performed on a man's abdomen which had been stabbed with a knife. The wound in the surgery was more than a span wide, and about two spans of the intestines had come out. The tear was in the middle of the abdomen.
He was treated after his intestines were protruding from the wound for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days. I continued to treat him until he recovered and lived many years afterwards, managing all his affairs. The doctors had previously judged that he would never recover at all.
It is astonishing that I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where there were no medicines at all. I would apply old cotton to the wound twice a day and attend to washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the types of special suturing, I mention it as per Galen's words: you begin the suturing from the skin and insert the needle from the outside to the inside. When the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle going straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
Left the edge of the peritoneum in the side where you inserted the needle and pushed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other edge of the pleura, so if you push it, push it again in this same edge of the pleura from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and push the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you push it through the peritoneum, reach the edge of the pleura in its vicinity until it penetrates entirely.
Then begin also from this same side and trace it with the edge which is of the peritoneum on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin that is near it, then reinsert it into that skin and trace the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the pleura.
And withdraw it from the skin that is on its side, and do this once again and do it repeatedly until you sew the entire surgery in that manner. The description of this suturing is to sew like the seams for fur.
To sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and to leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special suturing that Galen also mentioned, this is his exact words.
It is to sew it in the manner that some healers sew it, by bringing each part to its corresponding similar part naturally, joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the pleura to its other edge, which is done as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the side of the vein that is nearest to you from the outside, and pass it through to the inside alone, and leave the sides of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both sides of the peritoneum, then withdraw it also.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the other side of the vein on the opposite side, and this type of suturing is better than the general simple suturing which has been mentioned before. This is Galen's exact statement, and some have said that the abdomen can be sutured with two needles. This is done by inserting a single thread into both and starting by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side and passing it to your side according to the suture of the bishops, whether.
Know that if the wound is in the center of the abdomen, then suturing it is more difficult than other parts of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its treatment follows the rules of other wounds. That is, if you encounter the wound while it is fresh with blood before air alters it, and you bring back the intestines and suture them securely, then apply healing poultices to it.
If the wound has been altered by air, then apply some of the ointments you have until it pusces and the sutures fall out, and the peritoneum and veins heal. Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply old cotton when it begins to pus and change it twice a day as I have informed you until it heals. For in most cases, you will not need other treatments if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound may cause pain to the major organs.
It is necessary to dip a soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose fat and place it around the areas between the armpit and ribs, for if he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, which often occurs, then enjoin him with a cool astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound to its interior, and know that what is thick among the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin among them is harder to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from any surgery that occurs to it at all, due to the abundance and thickness of its veins, and the thinness of its wall and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. However, if what protruded from the wound was feces and you encountered it while it was fresh, then return it according to how you would return the intestine itself.
If a period has passed and it has turned green or black, it is necessary to tie it with thread above the place where it has turned black to prevent bleeding, for there are veins and arteries in the feces, then cut what is below that ligature.
You should make the ends of the thread attached below the surgery, coming out from it, to make it easy for you to pull and remove it when the feces fall and the wound suppurates. Mentioning the wound that occurs in the intestine, if there is a small tear in the intestine.
It may heal in some people, for I have seen a person who was stabbed in the abdomen with a spear, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. The wound became chronic and turned into a fistula from which feces and gas were expelled. I treated him, though I did not hope for his healing. I continued to care for it until it healed and the place closed. When I saw that the place had closed, I feared for the patient that something bad might happen inside him, but no bad thing happened to him at all and he remained in the best of conditions. He is healthy, eats, drinks, has intercourse, takes a bath, and exercises in his service. Some experienced people have mentioned that if there is a small wound in the intestine, it should be sewn according to this method.
It involves taking large-headed ants, then bringing the edges of the wound together, and placing one of these ants with its mouth open on the edges of the wound. When it bites down and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it will stick and not come apart. Then place another ant near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then close it and sew the wound. Those heads will remain stuck in the intestine until the intestine heals and is restored, without causing any harm to the patient at all. It is also possible to sew the intestine with a thin thread.
That which is taken from the tendons attached to the animal, after it enters the needle. It involves taking one end of this thread from the tendons and pulling it well, then tying a thin, twisted linen thread to its end, and then inserting that thread into the needle. In it, the tendon thread is used to sew the intestine, then it is returned to the cavity. This method of sewing with ants and tendons is done out of hope and wishful thinking.
And if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy for it and it will not heal at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the Treatment of Cold and Fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and old and turns into an ulcer and does not heal and continuously discharges pus without ceasing, is generally called a fistula, and we call it zakkam, the fistula.
It is in truth a hardened, white complex that does not cause pain and has a hollow like the hollow of bird's feathers.
For this reason, some have called it a feather, and it can be moist at times, continuously discharging pus. The moisture may cease at times; it can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
I have mentioned in the classification, when discussing surgeries, that every ulcer that does not heal and does not regenerate flesh is due to one of nine causes: one of them is either a lack of blood in the body or poor quality blood, or there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or it is due to excessive filth and corruption, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material discharging from it is of poor quality, or the treatment used is not appropriate for healing, or there has been a corruption in the locality similar to an epidemic, or there is something specific to the locality.
Or it is due to excessive filth and corruption, or the ulcer itself is putrid and the material discharging from it is of poor quality, or the treatment used is not appropriate for healing, or there has been a corruption in the locality similar to an epidemic, or there is something specific to the locality. As shown in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, and either because it has one bone or several bones, I have explained all these causes and their signs and treatments with medicines in the division.
I should here make you aware of the treatment of nasal polyps and the common cold which is performed by manual manipulation. Know that polyps may occur in all parts of the body, some ending in large veins or arteries or nerves.
or to the peritoneum or to the intestines or to the bladder or to a rib of the ribs or to a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back or to a joint of the complex joints such as the hand and foot, and some lead close to a major organ and so on.
Those polyps that have such characteristics are among the difficult-to-heal diseases that do not accept treatment, and dealing with them is tiresome and involves ignorance. If there are polyps that have not reached the areas I mentioned and you hope and expect to treat them.
And if you wish to ascertain that, take a probe of copper or iron if the polyp passes straight and examine it with it. If there is a bend in the polyp, examine it with a thin lead probe because lead can soften its body.
It will glide in upon entry and bend towards the curve. If the polyp has many openings that you cannot determine with a probe, inject one of its openings. The moisture you inject will follow the other openings and flow through them.
Then thoroughly examine in whatever way possible to know if there is bone or nerve, or whether the sinus is deep or close, or if it is a single sinus with many openings; ascertain all of this to the best of your ability by your subtle understanding and the presence of pain when you touch the area with your hand.
And similarly regarding the signs and causes leading to the swelling, if you ascertain all of this truly, then proceed with confidence to treatment, which is that you should consider whether it is.
If the sinus is apparent and close or in a safe location far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places mentioned to you, then incise the sinus as described previously and remove what is inside it of pus and corrupted flesh and excess tissue.
And what grows in its mouth of flesh or polyp and the like, treat it until it heals. If the sinus has a deep base and is straight, you should incise it as deeply as possible and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then use the medicated poultices with sharp medicines and insert them to the base of the sinus which you can reach with iron. Do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine consumes all that remains in the base of the sinus of corruption.
Then treat it with ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal thus, then cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the sinus is bone and this is confirmed to you, examine it and incise it as I have informed you, unless there is a reason preventing you from doing so.
If the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption or blackening in it, scrape it until its corruption is removed, then treat it with what heals it until it is cured. If it does not cure and the pus continues as it was doing.
Know that you have not reached the complete removal of corruption, so reveal all that is on it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort, then set it. If it heals, otherwise know that this corruption is in a deep place far from what is apparent, so reveal all that is on it again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with your utmost effort. If the corrupted bone is a small bone and you can pull it out, pull it out with the delicate forceps that are suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, thoroughly pull them all out and leave nothing. If something from the ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, cut those ligaments and the flesh if it is there, and free the bones.
If you cannot extract the bones immediately, apply to the wound what will cause putrefaction and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones rot. Keep the wound such that the incision you made during your work does not heal.
Rather, if you fear that, place cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments rot and the bones become clean, extracting and pulling them out will be easy.
If it is a large bone such as the leg bone or thigh bone or the like, and what has decayed is only its surface, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption are gone, then set the wound. If what has decayed is a large part, and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be spread open.
Cut it all until the corruption ends, then treat it until it heals. I inform you of a case of gangrene that affected a young man's leg to serve as an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young in age.
Around thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause that originated internally, moving until it affected the leg, causing swelling and a large lump. There was no external cause. Time passed with the mistake of doctors until the swelling ruptured.
Much matter flowed from it, and his treatment was mismanaged until gangrene set in, and many openings developed that discharged pus and bodily fluids. A group of doctors treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgical practice.
Until he came to me, and I saw his leg with matter flowing profusely from those openings. The man had become emaciated and his color turned yellow. I inserted the probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that they communicated with each other in all directions of the leg. So, I decided to incise one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone, which I found to be decayed, eaten away, blackened, and putrefied, perforated until the holes reached the marrow. I spread open what was revealed to me.
He is able to reach the corrupted bone, and I believe that there is no other corruption in the bone besides what I have cut out and exposed, and indeed I have excised it. Then I treated the wound with suturing medicines for a longer period, but it did not heal. So I returned and uncovered the bone again beyond the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I also exposed what of that corruption was apparent. Then I tried to set it, but it neither healed nor united. So I uncovered it again and kept cutting away parts of the bone bit by bit trying to set it, but it would not heal until I had cut off about a finger's breadth of the bone.
I removed it with its membrane, then treated it with medicines, and it healed quickly and was cured. This repetition in his treatment and incision was necessary due to the patient's weakness, his inability to endure much, and my fear for him of death because he would faint at times due to excessive vomiting. He was completely cured.
Flesh grew solid in place of the bone, his condition improved within his body, his strength returned, and he was able to move about without any harm affecting him while walking. If a protruding bone in part of the body has fractured.
It should be exposed in this manner: take a bandage and tie it at the end of the protruding bone, and have someone pull it upwards. Then use another thicker woolen bandage than the first one.
Then bind it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downward. As you expose the flesh in the area you wish to cut, ensure the saw does not harm the flesh. Place a piece of wood or a board firmly under the bone from below.
For if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone, and it is appropriate for the sawing to be slightly above the place of corruption so that there is no corruption within the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its exterior to the senses, thus forcing you to saw it again.
If the bone is corrupted but not protruding, and it connects to each other with the corruption in its center or part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides. Then place the wood at the bottom and saw from the first side where the corruption is.
When you finish sawing from one side, then saw from the other side, and let the sawing be slightly away from the corruption as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and saw the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the corruption is in the palm of the hand or the sole of the foot, treating it is extremely difficult, but you should scrape away the corruption however it appears to you, excise it, and clean it as much as you can.
And with what method can you proceed without encountering a vein or nerve, and know that the saws and cutting instruments for these bones are many according to the position of the bones, their setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and looseness. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare an appropriate tool for each type of work.
Know that the works themselves may guide you to the type of tool you need if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for skill in the craft comes from encountering various types of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to those for every disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter a number of instruments.
Make them examples to follow and standards to measure others by, if God wills. Image of a saw:.

Chapter Eighty-Seven.
On Amputating Limbs and Scraping Bones.
Limb parts may become gangrenous either due to an external cause or an internal one. If you treat this corruption with medication and the treatment is ineffective, and you see that the corruption is spreading through the organ without anything stopping it,
then it is necessary to amputate that organ where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great suffering worse than the loss of the limb. A sign of this condition is that the limb turns black until you might think that fire has burned it.
or it may become gangrenous after turning black until that gangrene spreads to the adjacent parts and affects the whole body; therefore, you should promptly amputate it. Similarly, if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some creatures such as the sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and the like.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not delay lest the corruption spreads to the hand. Similarly, if it has affected the hand, amputate it from the base of the wrist and do not delay lest the corruption spreads further to the arm's joint.
And if it is taken in the arm, then cut the arm at the elbow joint itself. If corruption continues and you see it spreading towards the shoulder, do not cut the shoulder at all, for that would lead to the death of the patient. Use other treatments as much as possible.
And do the same with the leg if corruption takes hold in the finger, then cut the finger at one of the joints. If it spreads to the foot, then amputate the entire leg. If it ascends to the knee, then amputate the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has spread above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and submit the patient to death. The method of amputating or incising a limb is to tie a band below the place you intend to cut and another band above the place.
One servant extends the lower band downward, and another servant extends the upper band upward. You should strip the flesh between the bands with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or incise. You should place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the healthy area, which could cause additional pain and a hot swelling for the patient. If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the area quickly or apply some hemostatic agents, then resume your treatment until you finish.
Then bandage the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you an example that happened to a man with this exact condition in his leg, which is that there was blackening with a burning sensation similar to fire.
And that corruption first occurred in his finger until it took the entire leg. The limb fell off from itself when he saw the corruption spreading in the member with the intensity of pain and burning that he was feeling, so he amputated it at the joint and healed.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption appeared in his ring finger. He came to me, and I treated that excess with the medications I applied to his hand after cleaning his body, but the excess did not stop and began to spread into the other finger.
Until the corruption spread to his hand, so he asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to stop that excess and also fearing for him that he might die if his hand were cut off, as his strength was failing. When he despaired of me, he returned to his country.
I was informed about him that his hand fell off, so he amputated the entire hand and healed. I have related this story to be of assistance in dealing with cases similar to this disease and to serve as a guide for reference and action.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medicines.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling continues until it gathers pus, then it bursts or breaks open and all the pus inside comes out, leaving the area empty as if it were a container.
And the skin on it is like a cloth that has become thin, and the corruption was not severe enough to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament. Therefore, it rightfully deserves to be called an abscess and should not be termed as a fistula unless its duration prolongs to the point where the corruption affects any of these organs.
Then it is called a naá¹£Å«r or zkÄma, and its treatment is to cut all of that skin as it goes around, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place due to its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and the corruption has not reached that extent, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. That is, look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus coming from it has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
It is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and zincumber in equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need, dilute it with water and honey, inject it into the abscess, seal its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract it by squeezing. Do this for days until the abscess is cleaned and the foul smell disappears. It may be injected with lye water if this medicine is not available, which is done by taking the ash of vine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject until you are certain that the abscess is cleansed. If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or lye water, inject it with a mixture of water and honey in which some powdered zincumber has been mixed.
Or inject it with a mixture of honey and decoction, for honey has the property of cleansing and washing, while the decoction adheres to the abscess, especially if the decoction has some astringent and drying qualities. If you do this several times and are certain that the corruption in the abscess is gone, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth.
Like taking the nakhli ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent syrup or inject some of the other medicines and ointments whose qualities we have described in the chapter on ointments, if the mouth of the wound is narrow and does not accommodate the syringe tube.
Widen it slightly with iron or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or sericum ointment until it widens. Similarly, if the mouth is also wide, bring its edges together with sutures and leave enough space for the syringe to enter without excess.
And if the mouth from which pus flows is raised upwards, cut it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, because if the pus congests in the depths of the wound, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the wound downward as desired, then position the limb in a way that allows the pus to flow out easily according to what is convenient for you, ensuring that no pus is retained within at all.
As for the healing medicines that are applied to the wound from the outside and how to apply them, take one of the healing ointments suitable for your needs and spread it on a piece of linen cloth. The cloth should be large enough to cover the entire wound. Then, cut with scissors a hole opposite the mouth of the wound slightly larger than the mouth of the wound. Then spread some of the soft ointment on another piece of cloth according to the size of the hole and place it over it, keeping it away from the mouth of the wound so that pus can easily come out. Do not remove the large cloth until afterwards.
Several days, and as for the one at the mouth of the wound, it should be removed at all times so that what has accumulated in it of pus may come out, and to know what flows from it of the suppuration whether it is much or little or ripe or unripe.
And also, examine the site of the wound itself whether its owner feels pain in it or not, and if there is swelling or no swelling. If your treatment proceeds this way and you see that the material which comes out is minimal at the mouth of the wound.
Know that the wound has adhered or is close to adhering. In this case, remove it tomorrow or on the third day. When you remove the dressing, replace the bandage and renew the ointment. If thin suppuration comes out of the wound after a long period, do not lose hope of its adhesion.
Its healing is near because among people, the growth of flesh in their wounds varies; for some it is slow and for others the opposite. If pus that is not ripe comes out of the wound after many days, know that the wound has not adhered. If the adhesion of the wound is delayed.
And if the condition prolongs, increase in drying the medications. Let the medications be such that they are moist in their composition but dry in their effect, like the palm ointment if more colocynth has been added to it. Among the most effective treatments is to use the palm ointment.
It is treated by applying rose oil and sprinkling on it a moderate decoction that is balanced in consistency, then knead it with it and use it. This can also be done if the palm ointment is not available: take honey and boil it until it thickens, then use it. Or you may take rue, aloe, and cyperus, crush them all together, and sprinkle the mixture on the honey while it is over fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bind it over the wound, or coat it with honey that has been cooked until thick, sprinkle the medications on it, place the cloths on it, and bind them.
Zeraunja longa, the root of the asphodel, cress seeds, and hellebore can be used individually or combined, grind them and sieve them, then sprinkle on the honey I described and use it. If the wound persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption deep within it.
And if it affects the bones or any of the solid nerves or ligaments, treat it as described for the treatment of the naá¹£Å«r previously mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On the treatment of corns and ingrown nails, amputation of excess fingertips, and splitting joined fingers.
A corn is an excess of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or toe and may also grow on other fingers. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells and becomes a hot tumor.
Or if it becomes infected until it erodes the base of the nail and may corrupt it entirely, or the corruption extends to the bone causing a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes widened with a bluish color. If treating it with the methods we mentioned does not succeed.
You should cut off all the excess that grows from the nail with an iron instrument, then cauterize the wound. Cauterization is very beneficial in this case. If the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy.
And the corner of the nail that has pressed into the flesh inward and caused it pain and harm, it is advisable to place a thin splint under the nail's corner which is hurting the flesh, raise it upwards, and cut that flesh gently.
And you apply to what remains of the flesh cauterizing, consuming medicines until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. And if the corruption has affected the bone, you should cut and remove that bone, for it will not heal as long as there is corrupt bone present at all.
If you see erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it at one of the joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or crush causing severe pain, you should first splint the injured area.
Then, you cut the nail with a sharp instrument, making an oblique incision from top to bottom, being careful not to reach the flesh underneath the nail, for doing so would cause severe pain to the patient and lead to excessive flesh growth in that area. Then, treat the area with what soothes the pains.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows on people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may contain bones in some parts, or it may even have a nail. Sometimes its growth originates at the base of a joint of one of the fingers.
Or its growth may occur at some joints of the finger. Those that grow at the joints of the finger do not move, while those that grow near the finger joint may move. What is purely flesh can be easily cut, and this is done by cutting it at its base with a wide instrument.
And as for those that grow at the base of the joint, their treatment is difficult, so one should avoid cutting them. As for those that grow in the finger near one of the salamiyat, it is appropriate to first cut its flesh in a circular manner towards the bone, then spread the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that occurs between the fingers, it often happens and can be something a person is born with or occur when a wound or burn from fire, etc., heals. It should be that this adhesion is cut open.
Until the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them a bandage or patches soaked in rose oil so that it does not adhere quickly, keep them apart or place a thin sheet of lead between them until the area heals as it should.
And similarly, if adhesion occurs between some of the fingers and the palm, cut open that adhesion according to what seems appropriate and suitable for the form of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the Cutting of Cysts.
Varicose veins are twisted, thick vessels filled with superfluous dark blood that can occur in most parts of the body, but they most frequently occur in the legs, especially in the legs of farmers, weavers, and carriers. It is appropriate to first use friction on the body with black powder several times with strong friction.
Then have the patient take basilic. As for treating it with iron, it is done in two ways: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other is to incise the vein and completely extract it. For cutting, first soak the leg in hot water thoroughly.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves. Then bind the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a turban. Then make one incision or two incisions or three wide incisions in the location.
Then remove the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards until the amount of blood that you deem sufficient or what the patient's strength can tolerate is removed. Then bind it and order him to avoid foods that generate black bile, and vomiting and bleeding should be repeated.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient, then this is how to proceed: Shave the patient's leg if there is a lot of hair, then have him enter the bath and wash his leg with hot water until it turns red and sweat is produced, or he exercises vigorously.
If a bath is not available to warm the limb, then cut the skin opposite the vein lengthwise either at its end near the knee or at its bottom near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and peel the vein from all sides until it becomes apparent to touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and prominent. Once freed from the skin, it resembles a tendon. Then insert a thread under it until it rises and protrudes from the skin, then secure it with a smooth blind hook. Then make another incision near that cut, about three fingers wide.
Then peel the skin off the vein until it appears, then raise it with thread as you did before and secure it with another smooth hook. Then make another incision or several incisions if necessary.
Then polish it and cut it at the end of the incision near the instep, then pull it out and polish it until it comes out from the second incision, then pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it out from the third incision, the highest of all, so that when it is all out, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and polishing, then insert a needle with a strong doubled thread and tie it, and pull it. Insert the awl under it and twist it with your hand in all directions until it comes out, and be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to polish it.
And if it causes harm to the patient, then after you have polished it entirely, place wool soaked in a drink of rose oil or olive oil on the site of the incisions and treat it until it heals. If the tendon is contracted and has twists in various directions.
And if it is not straight as we said, then it should be incised at every direction of its twists and places of curvature, then suspend it with forceps until you polish it entirely. And be careful when incising it that you do not cut the vein or injure it, for it will be difficult for you to polish it, so be very cautious.
Image of the instrument with which you incise the tendon:.
Image of the blind forceps:.
It should not have a bent like other forceps, nor should it be sharp at the ends to avoid injuring the vein. It should be thick in its curvature and smooth because if it is thin, it will cut the vein with its thinness; rather, its thickness is an advantage as we said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
On the Treatment of Civil Sweat.
This sweat develops in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and the lands of Arabs, and in hot countries that are slightly barren and less fertile. It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to corruption that occurs under the skin.
Worms and other pests can occur inside the bodies, as well as maggots and worms that develop between the skin and the flesh. A sign of the beginning of this sweat is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then that area discharges fluid, and then the sweat begins to emerge from where that discharge occurred.
It is as if it were the root of a plant or an animal, and when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing between one dirham and two dirhams, tied up, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
So, every time something comes out from it externally, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut off some of it and wrap what remains. Do not cut it off at its root before all of it has come out because if you cut it, it will retract into the flesh and cause a tumor, corruption in that area, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and drawn out little by little until it is completely removed from the body with nothing left inside. Sometimes, this sweat can produce something that is five or ten cubits long in some people.
I have been informed that it came out of a man to the length of twenty cubits. If it breaks off while you are treating him, insert a probe into its opening and pack it with a long plug attached to the body until all its material is discharged, and endeavor to cleanse the area with medicines for days.
Then treat it as you would treat tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing numerous openings to form, with each mouth emitting a branch. Treat it as we mentioned in the classification and according to what has been stated earlier.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On incising the worm generated under the skin, which is called the cattle disease.
This disease is called cattle disease in some regions because it often affects cattle. It is a small worm that develops between the skin and the flesh and moves throughout the body, moving up and down.
It can be sensed when it moves from one part to another until it penetrates the skin, creating an opening where it emerges. Its formation results from the putrefaction of certain mixtures, similar to how worms and snakes and melon seeds form in the stomach.
It is feared that if it moves within the body and rises to the head, reaching the eye, it may open there and emerge, thus destroying the eye, which often happens. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this can only be done when it is moving and its movement is perceptible.
It is necessary to tightly bind what is above and below it with a bandage, then incise over it and extract it. If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it, apply cauterization with fire to that area until it is burned. The greatest fear is its potential to damage the eye as we have said.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, tightly bind below it on the forehead, then incise over it and extract it. It is necessary to treat the patient by purifying his body with laxative medicines for the putrid and poor mixtures and to avoid foods that generate corruption.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On incising the disease known as nafir.
This disease which is called nafir in our country is a pain that occurs in some organs and then moves from one organ to another. I have seen it as I describe it to you.
I was called to a sick woman in one of the villages. She bared her arm and showed me a slight swelling in the brachial vein. After staying for an hour, I saw that swelling moving with the bone like a worm crawling upward towards her shoulder as quickly as possible, resembling quicksilver.
If it flows from one place to another, the pain leaves that place and settles in the shoulder. After sitting for an hour, it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm. She then told me that it moves through her entire body as I observed.
I was amazed by its speed in moving from one organ to another. I had not seen this disease with my own eyes in such a manner before, except that I have known some people who experience pain that moves from one organ to another, but I had never seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I did not appreciate this except that it might be because the woman was from the desert, with a dry body and visible veins. Therefore, this wind became apparent to the senses. Thus, it should not appear in this manner for those of affluence and moist bodies with hidden veins.
If you want to treat it and its owner feels pain, then if it appears before your eyes as we mentioned, bind above and below it quickly, incise over it until that congested wind comes out and dress the area.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with body purgation and what disperses gases and expels them, such as the stinking seed and the spikenard seed and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places where they lodge in the body. As for their difference according to their types, some are large and some are small. Some have hollow tips and some have solid tips. Some have three angles and some have four angles.
Some have barbs, and some have splinters. As for those that lodge according to the organs they enter, they are of two types: either they enter vital hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow falls into one of these organs and signs of death, which I will describe to you later, become apparent, then you should avoid extracting that arrow, for the owner will most likely die. And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not been concealed deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of brain injury is that if an arrow strikes it, penetrates the bone, and wounds the meninges covering the brain, severe headache, coma, and dizziness will result.
And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not been concealed deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of brain injury is that if an arrow strikes it, penetrates the bone, and wounds the meninges covering the brain, severe headache, coma, and dizziness will result. Redness in the eyes, a red tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, and sometimes blood coming out of the nostrils or ears, and sometimes speech is lost and the voice disappears, and from the site of the wound comes a white moisture resembling porridge, and a fluid similar to that of meat flows from it.
If these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and do not attempt to remove the arrow if it has not come out. As for the signs of the arrow when it strikes the heart and is close to the left breast, one feels as though it has embedded into something solid, not something hollow.
And sometimes the arrow may move in a manner similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting; know then that death is inevitable. The sign of the arrow when it wounds the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels near the neck swell, the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking to inhale cool air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it is close to the lower ribs, and breathing becomes labored with severe pain.
He sighs, and all the parts of the shoulders move. If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood resembling the color of the liver comes out of the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may come out of the wound.
And its condition is apparent, and if the arrow has pierced the abdomen and stuck in it, and some excrement has come out of the wound or fistula or a ruptured intestine, there is no hope for his treatment nor in extracting the arrow. If the arrow has hit the bladder and urine comes out.
And if something protrudes outward and the pain intensifies for the patient, then know that he is dying. As for other organs like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are usually spared in most cases.
When the arrow does not encounter a blood vessel or nerve and is not poisoned, I inform you of some of what I have witnessed regarding these arrows to guide you in treatment. This concerns an arrow that had struck a man in the nasolacrimal duct at the base of the nose.
I removed it from the other side under the earlobe, and he recovered without any harm to his eye. I also removed another arrow for a Jew that had struck him in the eyelid below the lower lid. The arrow had hidden itself, and I only reached its small end that attaches to the wooden shaft.
It was a large composite arrow from the qaswin, with a smooth square iron head and no barbs. The Jew recovered, and no adverse incident occurred to his eye. I also removed another arrow from a Christian's throat, and this arrow was Arabic.
It had barbs, so I made an incision between the laryngeal prominence and extracted it gently. The Christian recovered. I also removed another arrow for a man who had been struck in his abdomen, and we feared he would die from it.
When thirty days had passed or so, and there was no change in his condition, I cut along the arrow and maneuvered it out, and he recovered without any misfortune befalling him. I also saw a man who was struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound healed around it.
After seven years, the arrow came out at the base of his thigh. I also saw a woman who had an arrow embedded in her abdomen, and the wound healed around it; the arrow remained without any change in her condition, and she did not feel any harm in any of her natural actions.
I also saw another man who was struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound healed while he did not experience much pain. There are many similar cases. I extracted an arrow from a commander of the sultan that had struck the middle of his nose, slightly tilting to the right side.
The arrow was completely embedded, and I was summoned to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound extremely narrow and probed it with a thin probe but could not feel the arrow. He was experiencing throbbing and pain under his ear on the right side.
I suspected that the throbbing might be due to the tip of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a bandage containing a caustic substance, hoping that the area would swell and reveal the location of the arrow. However, no incident occurred in the area that indicated the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued to apply the bandage for many days, but no incident occurred. The wound eventually closed during this time, and the patient despaired of its extraction for a long period until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me. I then applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, feeling the thin tip of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow became visible to us, and I continued with it for a period of time, about four months.
Then when the wound widened and I was able to insert the forceps, I pulled and moved it but it did not respond to being removed. So I continued to apply various methods and tools until one day I grasped it firmly with some forceps, as will be shown in the figure at the end of this chapter.
The physicians had ruled that his nasal cartilage would not heal, but I set it and the wound healed, and the patient fully recovered without any further harm. I inform you of the method of removing some arrows so that you may use this as a standard and guide for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this craft and its details cannot be fully grasped by description nor encompassed by a book. Rather, the skilled craftsman measures the little with the much, and extrapolates from what is present to what is absent, and devises new work and new tools for unusual occurrences.
If I speak of this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the parts they have penetrated in two ways: either by pulling them out from the place they entered or from the opposite side, and those that are removed are taken out from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy area, in which case it should be pulled out. If it does not come out when you attempt to remove it, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it becomes putrefied, making it easier to pull out. The same applies if it has lodged in bone.
And if it does not come out for you, leave it for some days and repeat pulling and moving it every day, as it will come out. But if it still does not come out after several days, you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from all sides with a fine drill until you widen for the arrow, then pull it and remove it.
And if the arrow that has penetrated into the skull has deeply entered one of the brain's ventricles and some of those symptoms which I have mentioned appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling out the arrow and leave it until his condition improves after several days.
Proceed with pulling out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and does not come out when pulled, use drills around the arrow as described to you, then treat the area until it heals. As for if the arrow has become hidden in a part of the body. And if it is no longer perceptible, probe for it with a probe. If you sense it, pull it out with some tools suitable for pulling. But if you cannot due to the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there.
Make an incision to widen the wound and gain access to the arrow until you can remove it. If it has two barbs, grasp them and free the flesh embedded in them from all sides with any trick that you can manage. Proceed by cutting the barbs if you cannot free the flesh, twisting them until you do so.
And if you attempt to remove the arrow from any place, use the fingers of your hand with forceps in all directions until you free it, and be as gentle as possible so that the arrow does not break, which would make it difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot do this at the time.
Leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, then revisit it as it will be easier at that time. If bleeding obstructs you, use what we mentioned of treatment in its chapter, and be careful not to cut an artery or nerve or tendon, and employ cunning.
In whatever way you can free the arrow, do so gently, patiently, and steadily as described to you. It is advisable when pulling out the arrow to have the patient in the same position they were in when the arrow struck, as this is most suitable.
If that is not possible for you, use whatever shapes are available to you. As for the arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either part of it may protrude outward, or you may find the tip of the arrow by touch close to the top of the skin and see it protruding.
Make an incision over it, and let the incision be large enough to accommodate the forceps, then pull it out as it will be easier to remove. If it is stuck in the bone, use your hand in a circular motion until the arrow affects the bone and widens its path, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for days then revisit it until it comes out. If the shaft of the arrow is still attached, push it out with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use pushing, insert a hollow instrument so that its hollow part enters the tail of the arrow, then push it out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that fits into that hollow, thereby making it easier for the arrow to be removed. If the arrow is poisoned, you should excoriate all the flesh where the poison has penetrated, if you are able to do so, then treat it with what is suitable for that condition.
If the arrow is in the chest or abdomen or bladder or side, and it is close enough to be probed with a probe, and you can make an incision to reach it, then make the incision, being careful not to cut any vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it requires stitching, then treat it until it heals. Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:.
Its ends are like a bird's beak, crafted to grip the arrow or anything tightly so it does not let go. They can be made in various sizesâ€”large, small, and mediumâ€”depending on the size of the arrow and the width or narrowness of the wound.
Image of the hollow cannon:.
Image of the silenced cannon:.
This is blunted at the end like a probe to facilitate its entry into the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter Ninety-Five.
On Bloodletting.
The veins that are commonly bled from in the body are thirty veins, including sixteen veins in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the hassisin, the two superficial temporal arteries on the temples, and the two veins at the inner canthus of the eyes known as the naáº“áº“arin.
The vein standing in the center of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the two veins in the neck, the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins are known as the jugulars, and the two veins under the tongue.
And as for the veins that branch out in the arm and hand, they are five veins. One of them is the cephalic, which is on the ulnar side, and the common people call it the head vein. The median cubital, which is the middle vein, is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the body vein.
And the basilic, which is located on the medial side, and it is also called the armpit vein, and the common people call it the abdominal vein. The brachial artery, which is located on the elbow, is the one that adheres there and appears clearly above the thumb. And the axillaries.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, which has two branches. In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one is below the patella on the ulnar side, the second is the saphenous, located at the ankle on the medial side, and the sciatic vein, located at the heel on the ulnar side.
And in the other leg, there are three similar veins. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is in ligating them for chronic catarrhs, migraines, leprosy, and chronic bad sores on the head. The method of ligating them is as I describe.
It is to shave the head of the patient and rub its back where the two veins are with a coarse cloth well. Then, have the patient constrict his neck with a turban until the two veins appear and their location behind the ears at the two lower points on the head. Grasp them with your finger where you feel their pulse under your finger, and mark that spot with ink.
Then take a sharp knife, which is known as nashl, then insert it under the vein in the skin until the instrument reaches the bone; then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut off the vein along with the skin in one amputation, and the length of the cut should be about two fingers' width.
Or tie it, and let out as much blood as you desire, then secure them with sutures and leave them until they heal. Cutting and cauterizing them has been described at the beginning of this book. As for the arteries in the temples, their ligation is beneficial for chronic migraines, difficult headaches, persistent inflammation of the eyelids, and severe, constant tearing of the eyes.
And the method of their ligation, as I describe to you, is for the patient to tighten his neck with a headscarf until the veins appear prominently and their pulse becomes evident under your finger. At this point, mark them with ink, then lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger.
Then insert the nashl surgical instrument from below, lift the vein upwards, and amputate it as you did with the other veins. Let out blood according to your need, then loosen the patient's neck constriction and place your finger on the vein for a moment.
Then place cotton and a suture on it and tightly secure it from above, leaving it until it heals. Mentioning them, cutting them, and tying them has been described at the beginning of this book. As for ligating the forehead vein, its benefit follows ligation of the temporal vein for chronic facial ailments such as psoriasis, sores, and thick redness.
How to perform it as I describe to you: the patient chokes his neck with a turban until sweat appears, then take the tool which is called the ax, and this is its image:.
Place the sharp prong at the head of the ax on the same sweat spot and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and let the blood flow to the extent you desire, then loosen the patient's neck compression and tie it until he heals.
It can be opened with a wide scalpel, but the scalpel should not have a sharp point like other scalpels; rather, it should have a slightly broad tip. Apply it obliquely because the bone is close, and the scalpel might break if it is thin.
As for the sweat glands in the tear ducts of the eyes, their use is for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, trachoma, and facial diseases. As for how to perform bloodletting from them, it is that the patient should compress his neck with a turban, then you perform bloodletting while standing at his head, and let the bloodletting be slightly oblique towards the length with a small, slightly wide scalpel.
The area has no flesh, so if the scalpel is sharp at the tip, it might break. Then release as much blood as you need, place cotton on them, and bind them for one night, then remove the bindings. As for bloodletting from the nasal sweat gland, it is beneficial for acute fever.
And severe headaches and facial ailments such as chronic red boils that appear on the nose, especially if they are chronic. To perform bloodletting, the patient should compress his neck, then hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long scalpel.
You insert it into the center of the nose itself between the nasal septum in a straight line because the vein is not felt there, then the blood will appear immediately. You should penetrate with the knife slightly and let out as much blood as you need, then bind it for a night because it heals quickly.
As for the cheeks, puncturing them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and blackish diseases that appear on the skin surface, such as black leprosy, smallpox, and bad sores and ulcers. The method of puncturing them is to tie the patient under them in his neck with a band, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you puncture the vein lengthwise with a fairly wide puncture, then let out a moderate amount of blood or as much as you deem necessary. Then do the same with the other vein, and then remove the bandage.
And the veins are tied moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and leave it until the next day as the wound will heal. As for the veins of the jaw, puncturing them after puncturing the temple is beneficial for mouth ulcers, gum decay, bad sores, splitting of the lips, and bad sores.
Which are in the nose and around it. The method of puncturing them is to have the patient sit in front of you, tie his neck with a turban, then turn his lips aside and look at the two veins, one seen to the right of the lip and the other to the left, identifying them by their darkness.
That is because around them are thin black veins, so cut them off short. If you find it difficult to distinguish and do not know which they are, aim to cut the larger and clearer ones. Do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
And as for the two veins under the tongue, it is beneficial to lance them after lancing the temporal artery for goiters that occur in the throat due to tonsillitis and mouth diseases. The method of lancing them is to sit the patient in front of you facing the sun, raise his tongue, and look beneath the tongue where you will find a vein on one side and another vein on the other side, both having a color tending towards black.
You lance them and be cautious not to deeply cut them, for beneath them are arteries, and sometimes bleeding may occur from those arteries. As for the three veins that are lanced in the elbow, they are the ones commonly lanced for everyone, and their lancing is done in two ways.
Either with a broad mint-shaped needle or an olive-shaped one to thinness, or by incision with a knife-shaped needle, and these are the phlebotomy needles. [And this is their image.] Image of the broad mint-shaped needle:.
It should be broad as you see, suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, visible veins that contain thick blood like sediment. And this is the image of the olive-shaped needle:.
This needle is narrower and has a finer tip, suitable for lancing thin veins that contain thin yellowish blood. And this is the image of the phlebotomy needle:.
This phlebotomy needle is suitable for incision and comes in various widths, broad and thin, according to the width and narrowness of the veins. It can also be used as a reference for other instruments and is well-known among craftsmen.
And as for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of ligating it is that it draws blood from ailments that occur under the throat and neck, towards the chest and abdomen. The person who ties it should beware and be cautious because there is an artery beneath it; if he makes a mistake and goes too deep with the needle, he may cut this artery.
Bleeding may occur, so it should not be tied with a needle, but rather cut open with a scalpel. If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided and another vein sought or one of its branches found.
Or you can ligate the arm artery, as it is clearly visible and should be cut with a scalpel as we said. If you want to tie it specifically, you should feel the area before tightening the arm to identify the pulse location, then mark it with ink.
Then bind the arm and make a transverse incision in the vein with a scalpel as we said, ensuring that the cut is away from the artery's location. When you tighten the bandage and see swelling at the marked spot, know that this swelling is the pulsation of the artery; avoid it.
If you see the blood gushing out when you make the incision, like a boy's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. In this case, quickly place your finger on it for a long moment, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, it often does, then tighten the arm.
Leave it as is, and warn the patient not to neglect it; let it be secure without movement for several days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and you are overwhelmed and do not have medicine at hand, sever the artery if it is visible to you, as its ends will contract and the bleeding will cease.
Take the shell of a pistachio and split it, then take one half and bind it tightly to the site of the bleeding with bandages and ligatures until the next day. If the blood stops, otherwise treat it as previously mentioned by applying hemostatic agents, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Due to the small size of the wound and the ability to tie in the arm, inform him. As for the dark vein, the benefit of ligation is that it draws blood from the top of the head to the bottom of the body because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic as we said.
The person performing the ligation should do it on the neck of the vein being ligated, for beneath it are nerves. If he exceeds in puncturing with the lancet and hits the nerve, numbness may occur which is hard to cure, and sometimes does not heal at all, and this nerve often becomes noticeable to the senses.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not visible, you should make the ligation a split with a scalpel and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, cut the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of ligating it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein especially, if you want to ligate it, do so with stitches using an olive-shaped lancet or a broad basilic lancet because it is the safest of all veins, as there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet when ligating and seek a softer spot. It does no harm if it is not hit in the first attempt and ligation is repeated. However, it may swell in some people if it is not ligated on the first try.
But that swelling does not harm him at all, and as for the manner of bloodletting and its accidents and what should precede its correction, you should first know that bloodletting is either used for preserving health only.
Or it is used for diseases. That which is used for preserving health and sustaining it and protecting from the occurrence of diseases is to perform bloodletting in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, basilic, and brachial veins, and the bloodletting should be at the beginning of spring when the signs of fullness appear.
Bloodletting should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since daybreak. As for bloodletting that is used for diseases, it does not have a limited time but rather whenever the need and necessity arise, whether at night or during the day.
And at every hour and at any time, bloodletting should not be performed on children until they reach fourteen years of age, nor on elders who have surpassed sixty years. When anyone intends to perform bloodletting for any reason.
He should cleanse his bowels before bloodletting with a mild enema if there is much retained feces, so that impurities from the bowels do not adhere to the veins during bloodletting and harm the vital organs. One should not perform bloodletting on pregnant women, drunkards, or intoxicated individuals until those conditions have passed.
One should also avoid bloodletting immediately after sexual intercourse, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual activity, fatigue, exercise, insomnia, fasting, and anything that weakens the body or mind. Then, you should consider thinning the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
By foods and drinks and medicines if he can do so, then he enters the bath if nothing prevents him or exercises some exercise in order to thin the blood and let his veins be in the middle of the day as we said and aims to clear his chest that day from all evil mental conditions.
Such as worries, anger, fear, and all physical conditions like excessive fatigue, overexertion, sexual intercourse, and the like. Present in his environment are things that peopleä¹ æƒ¯ä½¿ç”¨çš„å„ç§é¦™æ–™ã€èŠ³é¦™å’Œå¨±ä¹ç­‰ï¼Œæ¯ä¸ªäººæ ¹æ®è‡ªå·±çš„èƒ½åŠ›è€Œå®šã€‚
Then the surgeon sits on a pillow that is higher than the pillow on which the patient sits. Then he extends his arm, and the surgeon scrapes it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with the clamp and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
For the tightening, when it is not moderate, either by excessive tightening it prevents the flow of blood, or if it leans to looseness, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after tightening, the patient should rub his hands together all over.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch. Then the surgeon wipes the lancet with a little old oil, then places his left index finger on the same vein under the spot he intends to cut slightly so that the vein does not escape and avoids the strike. For some veins are like tendons and escape when cut, and some are filled with air so that when the lancet is placed on them, they lower under the lancet and deceive the surgeon, who does not open the vein with the lancet, and even if he opens it, it would be narrow.
Therefore, the surgeon should be certain and deliberate in all these matters, then apply the incision. If the vein opens with that first attempt, otherwise he should repeat it slightly below or above that spot without delay if the area has not become swollen.
If it swells or the patient becomes anxious, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage as it may cause a hot swelling and he should avoid the bath. Then he can bleed again if desired. If the incision was deep and the opening small and the blood flow thin.
And if you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then reuse the incision in the same hole gently and straight, and slightly increase the opening. Do this quickly before the area swells, for in many people, the area may swell with a small incision.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat the incision at all as it will not help. Apply something from oil scouring, for it facilitates blood flow and is better in this case than the oil itself and other ointments.
Use oil scouring in all vein bleedings when blood flow is difficult. The Faruq antidote and shkhzniya can also be used; if either is applied to the area, the blood will become thinner and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of bleeding, it often happens, especially for those who have not been bled before or if the vein opening was small. Act quickly and place a sponge soaked in warm salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, as it will dissolve.
