It should be done after the blood has completely come out from the vein itself or another vein, and if there remains any blackness or greenness in the place after some days, that does not harm. If you wish, apply to it some dissolved asphalt and vinegar.
or some juice of cudweed and the like. Swelling and lump often occur when veins are opened, so place your hand on it; if you find it softens when you press it, then that is a bad lump, so beware of applying anything we mentioned to it.
For sometimes the blood of an artery may flow from it, but dress it with something that constricts so the area hardens, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. It should be that you draw from each person blood according to their strength and what appears dominant in the color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red. Similarly, if you see it thick, allow it to flow until it becomes thin. Likewise, if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides. It is appropriate for someone who is robust and strong and needs to have blood drawn all at once.
that the vein incision be widened and the lancet broad. For someone who is weak, the opposite should be done; the blood should be drawn in multiple sessions and the hole should be narrow. The best method for vein incision is to make a slanted, embossed cut, not stitched.
This method of bleeding is safe from excessive bleeding and nerve cutting, and it is better and more secure than bleeding by width or length. For those who faint during bleeding, you should give them some bread soaked in pomegranate juice or skenjbin before the procedure.
If he is feverish and blood has been let from him three or four times, then if his temperament is cooled, let him take before bleeding bread soaked in pymia drink or in sweetened honey drink scented with afwaieh or in a good-smelling herbaceous drink.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and its cause is the excessive discharge of blood, it is appropriate to give him meat broth and a light herbaceous drink, and to use the scent of galia, rubbing it on his chest, and to employ all that we have mentioned in the section on fainting.
Which results from vomiting, and as for one who wishes to relieve his arm and release blood again, it is appropriate for one whose bleeding was for excessive vomiting and whose strength is weak that the blood be released little by little according to strength over consecutive days.
And for one who wishes to relieve his arm and release blood again and whose body is strong, he should do so after seven or nine hours from the first bleeding. As for one who wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it has inclined, it is appropriate to relieve him on the second or third day.
And for one in whom the blood is abundant, having heated up and intensified and caused fever, it is appropriate to let out blood all at once and to extract a large quantity, widening the incision until fainting occurs, after being attentive to all the conditions of bloodletting. And place your hand on his pulse while the blood is flowing to prevent death instead of fainting, for this often happens if the bloodletter is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it should not be done if you intend to release the arm and let blood again.
And if the mouth of the vein is closed and it is difficult for the blood to come out, do not poke at it strongly or twist with force, as that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you can open it again or remove with a scalpel what of the blood has congealed in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply to it some salt that has dissolved in water, or apply to it some of the Farouk antidote or schyzandra and poke it gently until the blood comes out. If the vein has become swollen, then leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If there is a necessity to release blood again and it is unavoidable, then either open it above that spot or open another vein in the other arm or another vein.
As for opening the brachial vein, it is opened instead of the axillary and basilic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of both. The method of opening it is to have the patient immerse his hand in hot water until the wrist turns red and the vein becomes clearly visible, then bind above it with a bandage slightly in a moderate tightness.
Then open the vein slightly at an angle, neither across nor straight along, and let the incision be wide. Make your opening above the wrist joint slightly. If the blood does not come out easily, immerse the hand again in hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it meets your need. If you are in the summer days, you might not need to immerse the hand in hot water again. Most often, making the blood flow in hot water is done in winter. Opening this vein is safer than all other veins. For there is no harmful vein or nerve beneath it, and as for bloodletting from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of bloodletting is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after placing it in hot water until the vein swells.
For there is no harmful vein or nerve beneath it, and as for bloodletting from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of bloodletting is to tie the wrist with a bandage or with your hand after placing it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the sense, then you bloodlet slightly off-center, and even if you cut it entirely, that will not harm it at all. Be cautious; do not penetrate deeply with the lancet as there are nerves in the fingers and the area is bare of flesh. Then return the hand to hot water.
Leave the blood to flow through it, for if you do not return it to hot water, the blood will coagulate at the mouth of the vein and prevent flow. Once you have extracted the necessary amount of blood, apply oil and salt to the vein so that it does not close quickly.
You should do the same with every narrow branch. As for bloodletting from the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments. Proceed with its bloodletting in the same manner as the other. And bloodletting from the groin is useful for diseases in the lower body, such as uterus ailments.
And it is useful for the retention of menstruation, kidney diseases, chronic leg and thigh ulcers, and similar ailments. The method of bloodletting is to place his foot in hot water and massage until the veins dilate, then tie above the knee joint with a ligature, placing the vein at the ankle on the side toward the big toe.
It branches out into many branches on the leg, so bloodletting should be done in its widest branch or at the ankle where it converges, which is better and safer. If you perform bloodletting on the face of the foot, be cautious of the nerves beneath it.
Make your incision for it with a twist as if you want to amputate and the scalpel should be scraping. If the blood does not flow easily, let him dip his limb in hot water and allow the blood to run out until it is empty. If the vein is missed by the first attempt at bloodletting.
Let him return to bloodletting a little higher, for the area is safe and there is no fear from it if you are cautious of the nerves as we said. Similarly, you should do with the tarsal vein on the other leg. As for the sciatic vein, its location is as we said near the heel on the outer side.
The benefit of bloodletting for hip pain if it is due to hot blood, and the method of bloodletting is that the patient should enter the bathhouse and hurry to tie his leg from the hip up to four fingers above the heel with a thin long bandage, for it will not be apparent without that.
When it appears, perform bloodletting on whichever condition you stay, either with a twist, which is better, or cut it off completely or split it. Its location is safe and in most people, it is very hidden, so if you do not find it.
And if it does not appear at all to the sense, then bloodlet from its branches, which appear on the back of the foot towards the little toe and the ring toe, and be cautious of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you desire.
Then remove the bandage and place cotton on the site of bloodletting and secure the area, for it will heal quickly.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On cupping and how to use it.
Cupping vessels may be made of horns, wood, copper, or glass. Cupping is of two types: one with scarification and bloodletting, and the other without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is of two types.
And the cupping of the axillae, which are the sides of the neck from both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the ankles at the end of the heels, and the cupping of the elbows, which are in the middle of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the knees.
And cupping draws blood from the thin veins spread in the flesh. For this reason, it does not weaken as bloodletting does, and cupping should not be used, whether with fire or without, in any disease that arises from fullness until the entire body is purged.
When there is a need for cupping due to illness or as a routine practice, it can be performed at any time, whether at the beginning of the month, at the end, in the middle, or at any other time.
This is because there are people who, when they have an excess of blood that requires its removal through cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their head. Some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Others experience itching on their face and sides, and darkness and irritation in their eyes. There are also those who itch the area where cupping was performed.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouth and their gums bleed and they spit out blood, some sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, killings, and wounds in their dreams and the like. So when we see any of this.
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we recommend cupping after two or three hours have passed since noon. As for the benefit of the nahr cupping, it is beneficial for heaviness in the head and fluid dripping into the eyes.
However, it should be done after the entire body has been purged. This cupping canæ›¿ä»£æ”¾è¡€ï¼Œä½†é¡»æ³¨æ„ï¼Œé‚£äº›è„‘éƒ¨å¯’å†·æˆ–æ‚£æœ‰æ„Ÿå†’çš„äººä¸åº”ä½¿ç”¨æ­¤æ³•ï¼Œå› ä¸ºå®ƒä¼šç»™ä»–ä»¬å¸¦æ¥æžå¤§çš„ä¼¤å®³ã€‚
And therefore, it should not be used by the elderly and those who have cold diseases in their heads, and those who are addicted to it will produce forgetfulness.
And therefore, it should be ordered for the cupper to slightly lower his hand with the cup downwards to avoid causing forgetfulness, and as for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for bleeding from the temple and bleeding from the basilic vein.
And therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory organ, cough, and fullness. It should be slightly raised when cupping the shoulder because if it is made to go down, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for cupping the axillae, it is beneficial for pains occurring in the head, eyelid inflammation, migraine, choking, and pain at the roots of the teeth, and it is a substitute for bleeding from the basilic vein. It should be ordered for the cupper not to deepen his hand with the lancet so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding.
And as for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for cankers in the mouth, decay of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth, and it may serve as a substitute for bleeding from the veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
And as for cupping the sides of the loins, it is beneficial for what bleeding from the three veinsâ€”the basilic, the temple, and the median cubitalâ€”benefits, because it draws blood from all those thin vessels in the flesh, and these thin vessels draw from other thicker vessels until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper not to be excessive in cupping because the area is devoid of flesh and beneath it are nerves and blood vessels, and as for the single bleeding from the ankle, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores below.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper that the bleeding vessel should be large and made of brass because the area requires strong suction and perhaps a glass bleeding vessel might break and require much cupping. As for the cupping of the legs, it clearly reduces swelling.
Because it attracts blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, induces menstruation, and is helpful for boils and swellings. It serves as a substitute for cupping the temples and shins, except that it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. The benefit of cupping the shins is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of placing the cups is to first place the cup empty and suction moderately. Do not keep the cups in place for long but rather place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the mixtures can be evenly applied to the area. Continue repeating this process until you see the area turn red and open up.
When the redness of the blood appears, then you should cup again and gradually suction. Then observe the condition of the bodies. For those whose flesh is delicate and pores loose, it is advisable to cup them once only so that the area does not become ulcerated.
Instruct the cupper to widen the incision slightly and deepen it a bit, and to adjust the suction gently with a delicate movement. If the blood is thick, it should be cupped twice. In the first instance, open a path for the thin blood and its moisture, and in the second instance, to thoroughly extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, repeat the bleeding a third time to reach the limit. In general, if we want to let out a small amount of blood, one bleeding suffices; if we want to let out a large amount of blood, we perform more bleedings, and if we determine that the blood is thick.
It is necessary to make a deep incision, and the moderate limit for the incision is only the depth of the skin. What should be used with cupping are ointments, water, and what the person getting cupped should avoid. As for those whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and has narrow pores.
It is recommended to apply opening, soothing, and dissolving ointments on the areas where cupping will be performed. If it is summer, use ointments like that of khiryi or violet oil or sweet almond oil or pumpkin seed oil.
If it is winter, use ointments like narcissus oil or lily oil or chamomile oil or hyacinth oil and the like. If the waste matter is thick and cold, then the ointment should be marzunjoosh oil or nightingale oil or cowbane oil or sage oil and the like.
If the person being cupped has wide pores and tender flesh, it is best to avoid using ointments. These individuals should wash the cupping marks after the procedure with rose water or cold water or foxglove water or pumpkin water or purslane water and the like. As for those whose blood is very moist.
He should wash the cupping marks with vinegar or water of wormwood and buckthorn and the like. As for those whose excess matter is thick, they should wash the cupping marks with old wine or marzunjoosh water or sage decoction or chamomile and the like. It is advisable to avoid cupping in the bathhouse and immediately after bathing.
It should be used after exiting the bath by one or two hours and it is not appropriate for anyone to sleep after cupping. What should be managed with the cupped and bloodletting before and after cupping.
You should first look if the one who is to be cupped or bloodlet has a bilious temperament and if his blood tends towards sharpness and inflammation, then he should take cooling things like pomegranate, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, sorrel, and similar juices. His meals should consist of light dishes, lamb broths, legumes, and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honey syrup or sorrel vinegar drink or burdock sorrel, and he should consume moderate aromatic wine that is between old and new. He should be advised to reduce his food intake, and his meals should consist of light dishes, quail, sparrows, and young pigeon stews. The drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting should be more than food.
It may be appropriate to give some people in certain times the Farooq antidote or musk medicine or salvia before cupping and bloodletting or after it to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood. It should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for the cupping that is done without scarification, it is the cupping that is applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and hip joint because these organs cannot tolerate scarification. The purpose is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Like placing the cups on the breasts for the condition of sneezing, or using them to expel a cold air that has lodged in an organ, such as placing the cups on the abdomen and navel. This loosens the organ, warms it, and relieves pain by expelling that air.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stone, for by its strength in pulling, it may open the blockage or remove the stone from its place. It also does this when placed on the liver and spleen when there is wind that becomes turbulent within them. These leeches can also be used empty by suction only.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water for ailments of the eyes. This is done by filling the leech, let it be large, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for this have been boiled.
Then it is placed filled on the area, held in place, removed, and repeated as needed, and this is a picture of the leech that is used with fire:
Its mouth should be wide enough to fit two fingers as we have depicted, and its depth should be about half a finger-breadth. There should be a small hole on the side, approximately in the middle, through which an needle can pass. It is made of Chinese brass or yellow brass with thick edges.
It should be smooth, flat, and polished so that it does not harm the organ when placed on it. There should be a cross channel in the middle made of brass or iron where the candle for heat is placed. This leech can also be made larger than described or smaller according to the disease.
And regarding its use, the leeches for children and thin individuals are different from those for men and thick bodies. As for how to place this leech with fire on the organ, it is done by lighting a wick from fine linen or a small candle made of wax and placing it on the middle rod that is in the center of the leech.
The fire should rise upwards towards the bottom of the cup to prevent the patient's body from burning. Then it is placed on the limb, with a finger over the hole mentioned until we grip the cup and no longer need it, then remove the finger.
The steam comes out through that hole and the cup is loosened in its place. Then light the wick as described and repeat if necessary. As for the cup used for treating hemorrhoids with water, it has no rigid rod or hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it on the limb only, and this is its shape:.
And this cup is better when it is large to hold a lot of water.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On Attaching Leeches.
Leeches are mostly used in parts where cups cannot be placed, either because they are small like the lip and gum, or because the limb is devoid of flesh like the finger and nose and the like.
To use them, select leeches from pure fresh water free of contaminants, then leave them in fresh water for a day and night until they are hungry and there is nothing left in their bodies. Then the body is first bled by venesection or cupping, then the affected limb is wiped.
Until it turns red, then place the leeches on it. When they are full, they will fall off. If possible, cupping the area is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, massage and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off and this is a discharge, soak a piece of linen in cold water.
Place it from above until the discharge stops. If the blood flows abundantly, sprinkle on it powdered zaj or turpeth or something similar that constricts until the blood stops, or apply peeled and split fennel to the area.
Leave it until the fennel adheres to the area, for the blood will stop. If leeching needs to be repeated, do not use that same leech if possible to use another one. If the leech refuses to attach, then wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or prick a needle in the area until some blood comes out, then place [the leech]. For when it senses some blood, it will adhere to its position. If you want to remove it, sprinkle on it some aloes or salt or ashes; it will fall off easily.
The Third Chapter.
On Fractures.
This chapter also deals with the skills required in the art of medicine, which is setting and treating fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by ignorant physicians and laypeople who have never browsed through an ancient book on the subject or read a single letter from it.
Because of this reason, this branch of knowledge has become non-existent in our country, and I have never come across anyone who is proficient in it at all. What I have gained from it, I have gained through extensive reading of the books of the predecessors and my dedication to understanding them until I extracted that knowledge from them. Then, I persisted in practice and training throughout my life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience with it has passed after I brought it close to you, freed it from the pitfalls of lengthiness, condensed it as much as possible, clarified it to the utmost, and presented many illustrations of the tools used in it.
For it is an addition to the clarification as I did in the previous two chapters, and there is no power except by Allah the Most High, the Great.
The first chapter.
Since there are summaries and key points regarding the matter of broken bones that should be presented, before we begin to discuss each fractured or dislocated limb individually, it is appropriate to mention some sayings and sections at the beginning of this chapter. First, they will compel you to understand them and grasp their true nature, you and whoever is eager to learn this noble craft besides you.
So I say, whenever a fracture, dislocation, swelling, or fall occurs to someone, you should first quickly address his bloodletting or purging, or both. Unless there is a hindrance such as weakness, or the one who experienced it is a child or an old frail person, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, limit his diet to cold legumes, the meat of birds and kids, and avoid drinks, heavy meats, overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood until you are certain there will be no swelling and do not expect any substance to flow to the affected area.
Then the patient should return to his usual regimen. When the broken bone begins to heal, the patient ought to be fed food that is thick, substantial, and strong withç²˜æ€§å¦‚å¤§éº¦ã€ç¨»ç±³ã€è±†ç±»å’Œç‰›è‚šã€‚ And eggs and soft fish and thick drinks and the like, for with this regimen the healing of the fracture will be faster and better. Know that if the broken bones are in strong men and elders, they cannot reconnect and heal to their original nature ever.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, what was extremely flexible, like the bones of small children, may connect and heal, but nature grows something on the broken bone from all sides that resembles glue in thickness, adhering to it and strengthening it until they adhere to each other.
It binds them together until it reaches the utmost strength and security as it was initially, so that nothing hinders its actions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should include foods that are strong, adhesive, and thick, as we have said. Know that fractures differ in type according to the different parts of the body, for a leg bone fracture is different from a skull bone fracture, and a chest bone fracture is different from a spine bone fracture.
And so it is with all other parts of the body, each differing from one another. We will come to describe each type of fracture in detail in its own section, separately from the others. The type of bone fracture may also vary on its own.
For it may be that his fracture is splintered without shards occurring in it, and his fracture may be along the bone, and its fracture has shards and projections, some protruding and others not, and the fracture occurs with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
For each type has a specific method in setting it, as will be detailed in its place. Among the signs of bone fracture are its bending, confusion, visibility to the sense, and its crackling when you touch it with your hand.
When there is no visible bending, no crackling, and you do not feel any disturbance when you touch the bone, and the patient does not experience much pain, then there is no fracture; it may be a sprain or a slight bruise or a minor crack. Therefore, it should not be moved by stretching or pressing at all.
Rather, apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later, then bind it gently. Know that if the bone is broken and split into two without shards occurring in it, each part has moved away from the other.
You should promptly set it and align it before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it as gently and skillfully as you can.
Know that setting and aligning it is easier than with a bone in which shards have occurred. Then, bind it as will be detailed later. If the bone has shards, it is necessary to stretch the broken limb from both sides, whether an arm or a leg.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with the rope and hand. Place the limb on a flat surface in its natural shape so that when part of the broken bone extends, then reduce those protrusions to their places with all the cunning and gentleness you can manage.
Strive to ensure that your actions do not cause pain or discomfort to the patient. Aim to align one bone with its counterpart in the best possible position, and during this process, touch and feel them with your hand. If you notice anything amiss, correct and adjust it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and strong poking, as many unskilled people do. Often, their actions cause severe swelling or deformity in the limb, which I have observed from their practices on multiple occasions.
After alignment, perfection, and securing, keep that limb still and at ease. Instruct the patient not to move it whether awake or asleep, during turning or disturbance, during defecation, or in all movements as much as possible, and ensure its position is one where pain is avoided.
For if he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position where he does not feel pain, and at the same time, ensure that its position is flat and straight.
To prevent deformity in the limb when it heals, and as for how to secure a broken limb, I will describe it to you. Know that broken limbs differ in size and shape; those that are small, such as the arm, finger, ankle, and the like.
It is necessary that the bandages be light and gentle, but for those areas which are tough like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be broad and strong because the wide strap holds the large limb securely, binding it equally on all sides without any imbalance.
Then after you finish leveling, you should apply the corresponding paint. With a gentle pressure on the site of the fracture, you begin to wrap the bandage around the fracture itself three or four turns, depending on what the limb requires. You slightly tighten the bandage with your hand and then go up to the upper side of the fracture site.
With less tension than you applied to the fractured area, you then move the wrapping slightly away from the fracture site and gradually loosen the tension little by little until you achieve a proper fit for the correct position. Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
After the turns, you move the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture. Your action in tightening and loosening the wrap should be as we mentioned for the first upper wrap. Then place some gentle padding or cloth between the wraps to straighten out the deformity of the fracture.
If there is a deformity, then place something in it to correct it; otherwise, do not add anything. Then wrap another bandage over it and apply the tight splints immediately on these wraps if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something to reduce the swelling and eliminate the inflammation. Leave it for a few days, then apply the splints at that time. The splints should be made of broad, carved, and wisely prepared half-reeds, or they can be made of colander wood.
Which is made from pine or palm fronds or reed or linen and the like that are available of these, and let the width of each splint be in this form and exactly this shape.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what is appropriate for the size of the broken limb, the form of the splint:.
Then bind the splints with another bandage according to the same tightness as your first binding, then secure it from above with tight threads according to what we mentioned about the tightness, which is that your binding at the site of the fracture should be tighter. And the further away from the fracture, the looser the binding should be.
The threads should be of moderate thickness and fineness, and they should preferably be made of wet linen, because if the threads are too thick, as I have seen ignorant people do when they make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because binding with them leads to excessive tightness, and very thin threads are also unsuitable because you will not achieve the desired tightness with them. There should not be less than a finger's width between each splint.
If the patient is bothered by the edges of the splints after they are correctly tightened, place soft padding or loose wool under them so that it does not cause any discomfort. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and there is a break in the skin, we will address this separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be bound with splints from the first day. This is because if the limb is large, splints should not be applied to it until after five or seven days, or more, according to your assurance of the absence of hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages which the ancients used for fractures, dislocations, and wounds. A general brief description of a bandage that fixes fractures and suits most temperaments, especially children and women, as it does not excessively heat or cool.
Take the flour dust from the mill, which sticks to the walls of the millstone when the mill is in motion. Knead it without sifting, using egg whites, making sure the dough is neither too thick nor too thin, then apply it.
Description of another bandage for fixing fractures, dislocations, and wounds: Take marshmallow, alum, atractylis, rhatany, wool, and sugarâ€”ten drachms of each. Also take vinegar and honeyâ€”five drachms of each. Additionally, take willow twenty drachms, and Armenian or Roman clay twenty drachms. Grind all the ingredients and mix them with willow water or egg whites if the patient's temperament is hot. Use this bandage, as it is well-composed, quickly fixes broken bones, and suits most people due to its balance.
Description of another bandage that is also beneficial for fractures and wounds: Take wool, marshmallow, and white colocynthâ€”ten drachms of each. Also take vinegar and honeyâ€”five drachms of each. Add atractylis six drachms, and Armenian clay twenty drachms. Grind all the ingredients finely, sieve them, knead with water or egg whites, and apply.
Description of a bandage for joints and bones that have moved from their places, which alleviates the pain associated with them, and for bone fractures and breaks. Take white wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, then place it on the affected area. This bandage does not have healing power.
However, it is excellent for reducing hot swelling and alleviating pain particularly. Description of another bandage that mends broken bones: take dark fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind them together, and apply them moistly. Description of another brief bandage to use when mending a bone fracture.
And when you want to reduce the remaining swelling, take equal parts of the root of calamint, chamomile, purple violet, and ground cyperus rotundus. Grind all together and knead with egg white if the limb is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, then knead with coriander water or plain water and use it.
Description of another bandage that is stronger for reducing swelling, used when solid swelling occurs during the mending of a bone. Take equal parts of the root of calamint, flaxseed, fenugreek, marigold, martynia, purple violet, and chamomile.
Grind all together and knead with decoction water or fresh water or egg white, according to the heat of the limb and its cooling. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that anything should be placed on the broken limb when mending it.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil alone, and it should be described as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he advised that things with a drying quality along with some warmth, such as salt, opoponax, and frankincense, among others we have mentioned, should be placed on the broken limb when mending it.
A description of a bandage that is beneficial for weakness and pain: Take wood ashes, chickpeas, human hair or bird feathers, colocynth, and salt in equal parts. Pound them, sieve them, knead them, and apply them as a bandage. As for the amount of time the bandage should remain in place.
Then it is to be left in place, for you should observe if there is no pain or itching in the injured area and the broken bone does not move from its position; then do not remove it for many days. If there is severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling in the area, then it should be removed.
Hasten to remove it at that time and do not delay in the slightest, then remove the bandage. Take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside, then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply clean wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat, bind it on for a night to prevent inflammation and to subdue the swelling and pain of the limb. Afterwards, return it to gentle binding with a light bandage without tightening it as you did initially.
Tenderly care for it until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and all other symptoms have completely disappeared and you need bandaging and binding again, then repeat as you did initially. If none of the mentioned issues occur in the limb.
Do not remove it until three, four, five, or seven days have passed; it may even be left for twenty days. All of this depends on the condition of the limb as you observe it, as we have said, until the break is secure and the flesh is close to healing over it.
Also increase the tension more than your first tightening altogether, and also increase the thickness of the patient's diet as previously mentioned. If you see that the site of the fracture has dried out and become thinner than it should be, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
So treat it with lukewarm water every time you change the dressing, and let that be done every three days. Slightly reduce the tension, for by this action, nourishment flows to the member and it heals quickly. As for what the ignorant among the bone setters do, which is to break the member again.
If it does not set properly at first and sets crooked, then that is an error in their action and a great deception. Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted accordingly. I have never seen any trace of that from any of them at all. And the correct approach is not to follow it.
The Second Chapter.
On incidental fractures in the head.
Incidental fractures in the head are diverse, their forms are different, and their causes are varied. Some fractures result from a sword blow and may involve the complete separation of the bone until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
This type of fracture is therefore called an axe-like fracture. Or, the sword may cut part of the bone and only separate its surface without penetrating to the end, which is called an avulsion fracture. The wound of these fractures may be either large or small.
And there are fractures that become crushed or shattered, and their cause is a blow by a stone or falling onto a stone or the like. This type of fracture either penetrates close to the membrane beneath the bone or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound of these two fractures can also be either large or small, and there are fractures that are hidden in the bone at the fineness of hair, which is a slight crack.
For this reason, this type of fracture is called hairline. Among them are fractures caused by a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, where the surface of the bone indents inward, making the area concave, as seen in a copper pot when struck, part of it indenting inward.
This is most common in fresh bone heads, such as the heads of children. All these types of fractures may have disassociated and undisassociated fragments, and we will discuss the treatment of all of them in their respective places. We identify all these types of fractures through examination.
Examining it with probes and removing the rotten flesh from it. As for the hairline type, it is identified by examining the bone, wiping it, and applying ink, then the fracture will appear black. As for treating the fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient.
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, protruding and red eyes, and other similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat them, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
And if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then at that time begin his treatment. This is the case when the wounded comes to you right after being wounded, and this happens in the winter days.
It is necessary to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is in the summer days, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day so that what is under the bone of the membrane does not spoil, thus causing those symptoms we described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and is accompanied by fragmentation and bruising, it is necessary to cut away the fragmented and bruised portion as I describe to you, which is to shave the head of the injured patient.
And expose the bone in whatever manner is feasible for you, according to the shape of the wound and what is easier on the patient. If bleeding or a hot swelling appears when exposing the bone, handle it as appropriate.
This involves stuffing the area with cloths soaked in a mixture of wine and rose oil, and cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is controlled. Then proceed to set the bone and extract it, which can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a sharp, narrow-bladed instrument, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter after this one, slightly wider, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter, even wider than the second. It is necessary to have a set of different cutters, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, with extremely sharp edges. They should be made of Indian iron or good steel.
Use gentleness in tapping on the fractured area so as not to displace the head and harm it. If the bone is strong and solid, you should pierce around it before using the saws with drills which they call non-penetrating drills and these are named non-penetrating drills.
Because it does not exceed the boundary of the skull bone to what is beyond it, as the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle that prevents it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should have a variety of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have a drill whose sharp end length and size match the thickness of that skull. This is the image of three types. Of the drills, there is a large, medium, and small:
As for the method of drilling around the broken bone, you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then move the drill to another location, making sure there is a distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a thread or similar.
Then, use the saw to cut between each pair of holes and do this with utmost gentleness as we have said until you remove the bone, either by hand or with another tool from among the instruments you have prepared for this purpose.
Such as glue and delicate forceps. You should be extremely careful that the drill or saw does not touch any part of the membrane. If you have removed the bone and it has healed from the membrane, which was attached to it, and you have extracted it, you should then clean it thoroughly.
And smooth out the roughness that remains in the bone with another tool similar to the saw, except it should be thinner and more delicate than other saws. If any small bones or splinters remain, gently remove them with the tools you have.
Then treat the wound with bandages and ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other aspect of the work, it is very simple and far from harm, as Galen mentioned and greatly praised, and this is his saying. You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and noticeable. Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lens-shaped saw under it, and this is its shape:
The lens-shaped part of it should be smooth and not cut anything, while the sharp part is on the sides extending in length as you see, so that the lens-shaped part rests against the periosteum and the sharp face of the saw is against the bone. Then strike the saw from one side.
With a small mallet until you cut through all the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from any harmful incident in the membrane, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and timid of people. Indeed, even if he were drowsy.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some areas, scrape it off with the edge of the lens-shaped saw itself and remove it gently, for it can be removed without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane, and only the surface of the bone is broken with roughness and small splinters remaining, you should remove that roughness and extract those splinters with delicate chisels that have been made in various sizes so that you can use the most appropriate and suitable one for each area according to what the work guides you to.
And the nature of the fractured bone and its shape, and it is appropriate that you first use for scraping the bone the widest of those scrapers, then use after it a finer one than it, and continue doing this successively until you reach using the finest and thinnest of them all.
And as for other small, superficial cuts and delicate fractures, it is appropriate to use treatment for each one according to what leads to its improvement, which is something not hidden from anyone with even the least skill in this craft and who has understood what we have written and explained about large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, it is appropriate to take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil, then place it on the mouth of the wound. Then take another folded or triangular cloth. And soak it in a mixture of rose oil, then place it on the first piece of cloth. Do this as gently as you can so as not to burden the intestines. Then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it beyond what is necessary to hold the wound in place only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain that the heat has subsided, then remove it and use thereafter medicines that have a drying quality, such as the roots of lilies, crushed cyperus, powdered millet, and zeraunja, and the like. This is done by making a powder from these medicines.
And sprinkle the powder on the wound while it is dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses without irritating. Strive to keep the treatment of the wound clean, with no foul-smelling oil or dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate at all.
For the pus, when it gathers on the meningeal membrane, spoils and corrupts it, causing a great calamity to the patient; and it may occur in the cerebral membranes when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If there is a blackness on its surface, and you observe that the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, then know that he is surely doomed. If the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied to it, and that medicine was potent enough to cause such blackness.
You should take a portion of honey and three portions of rose oil, mix them well, soak a cloth in the mixture, then apply it to the wound, and continue treating it with appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of the nose if it is broken.
Know that only the upper parts of the nose, both or one of them, can break because they are made of bone. The lower part is cartilaginous and does not break but may suffer from swelling, deformity, and indentation.
If one side of it is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and align the fracture from inside while using your index finger and thumb to manipulate it from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this gently and be careful not to cause pain to the patient with your actions.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, you should straighten it with a slightly thickened rod. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. It is advisable to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert into the nostril's hole a wick of cotton cloth if the fracture is on one side or insert two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril's hole, and some of the early experts in setting fractures mentioned that the wick should be soaked in lard and changed every day. I do not agree with that; rather, the wick should be soaked in egg white mixed with millstone dust.
Then leave the wick until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens. Feather quills of ducks can be inserted into the nose in place of the wick, wrapped with soft cloth to better support the nasal fracture and to ensure the patient can breathe.
This is not necessary, if you choose to do it or make the wicks instead. If a hot swelling appears on the nose during your treatment, dress the nose with kerotyo or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some ointment of diachylon.
If no hot swelling occurs, it is advisable to dress it externally with semolina flour and bulgur wheat that has been mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it. Do not bind the nose at all. If the nasal bones are fractured in small fractures.
Or if they are shattered, it is advisable to incise and remove them with a suitable instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and suppuration. If there is a wound inside the nose, it should be treated with wicks and lead pipes should be used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the repair of the lower lip if it is broken.
If the lower lip is broken and there is no wound with the fracture, you should examine it. If the fracture is only from the outside and has not split into two parts nor caved inwards, then its treatment is easier.
It is advisable that if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, you should insert the index finger of the right hand and gently raise the raised part of the fracture from inside to outside.
Your other hand should control it from the outside to straighten it. If the jaw fracture has split into two, it is advisable to pull from both sides in a straight line until it is aligned. If the teeth have become misaligned as a result, they must be corrected.
Or if they are separated, sew them together with a thread of gold, silver, or silk where you hope it will stay. Then apply kerotike to the broken lip and place a folded cloth over it. Over the cloth, put on a large, secure splint or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the lip. Then tie it from above according to what seems appropriate for binding and keeping it together so that it does not come apart. Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and give them soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect any change in shape has occurred, promptly untie it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what seems appropriate for binding and keeping it together so that it does not come apart. Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and give them soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect any change in shape has occurred, promptly untie it on the third day. Then you should prepare what you bandage with mill dust and egg white or with semolina flour after removing the lint from it, and place a soft cloth over the bandage. As long as that bandage sticks to it and no change occurs in the bone's condition, leave it.
Do not remove it until it heals and the fracture strengthens, for this fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If during that time a hot swelling appears, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to alleviate it until the swelling disappears.
And if the fracture is with a wound, examine it. If a fragment or fragments of bone have become detached, gently extract those fragments in a way that suits their removal from the wound. If the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, after you have extracted those fragments and nothing remains, suture the opening of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. Suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the ribs if they are fractured.
Ribs most often fracture from the front, near the end of the shoulder blade. Their fracture can occur in one of three ways: either they break and collapse without producing fragments, which is the easiest to set.
Or fragments may be produced in the fracture, which is more difficult to set. Or the fracture may occur with a wound. If the fracture does not involve a wound, prepare two assistants: one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured rib, and the other to extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, without any protrusion or indentation. If more extension is needed, you should place a ball of cloth or wool under the armpit of the patient.
Its bone should be according to your need, extend and raise the splint and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is aligned as it should be. If you cannot pull the end of the splint out because it has gone deep, then the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately thick pillow is placed under his armpit, and the servant presses his shoulder downward until the end of the splint that has gone deep rises up. Then align and correct the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the bone has broken off and is moving, you should incise over it and gently remove that splinter. If the splinter is lodged in the bone, you must devise a way to cut it with one of the saws prepared for this purpose. After placing under the splint the device that protects the intestines, which is made of wood or iron, and this is its shape:
It resembles a spoon without any indentation. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. Its length should also be suitable for the task at hand. It should have two ends, one end being wider and the other narrower as you see.
If the incision you made to remove the bone splinter is wide and you are not concerned about a hot swelling, then suture the edges of the wound together. If the incision is small or you fear a hot swelling, then pack the wound with cloth and bandages according to the cut of the wound.
If a hot tumor appears, then soak the incision in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting the bone when there is no cut or incision, it is to apply to the bone a dressing made from mill powder mixed with egg white. Place the soft splint on it, then put a ball under his armpit if needed. Then take a turban that is very long and about a finger's breadth wide or so. Then apply a folded pad on the splint and dressing. Then take a splint from a thin board. Its width should be three fingers and the same in length. Then insert it into a cloth, then place that cloth with the splint in the part of the turban that falls over the fracture site, then tighten the turban around the fracture as you go around. Place it around his neck, under his healthy armpit, and under his injured armpit, wrap it several times on each side, and adjust the tension as you see fit to secure the fracture firmly, which is not something that would escape your notice.
The goal of all this is to ensure that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day. Whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on their back and place a small pillow under their armpit when they sleep at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, thus raising the clavicle fracture with the elevation of the shoulder, or bind his arm to his neck. Do not remove the bandage unless an incident occurs at the site, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the dressing if you see a need for it. Tie it tightly and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and becomes stronger, which takes about twenty-eight days. And in some people, it may take less time than that.
Chapter Six.
On the healing of a shoulder fracture. The shoulder rarely breaks at its broad part, but rather its ends may break. Whenever a part of it breaks or it breaks in the middle, this can be known by touch. The treatment should be according to the form of the fracture.
Straighten it and return it to its natural shape by whatever means possible, then apply the powder of the millstone with egg white and soft wax to the area. Place a folded cloth pad on top, then secure it with a wide board splint.
Make it thin enough to cover the entire shoulder or slightly wider. If there is an indentation under the splint in any part of the shoulder, fill that indentation with soft wax until the splint lies flat. Then secure it from above with a long bandage.
Tie it firmly and ensure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever it loosens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it moves. The patient should lie on their sound side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
It takes twenty or twenty-five days. Once this period is complete, remove the bandage with confidence, as it is one of the bones that does not fear fracturing or collapsing. If a splinter appears from the bone and pierces under the skin, cut over it and extract it. And make what I mentioned for the fracture of the clavicle to soothe the hot swelling if any of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of chest fractures.
The chest may fracture in its center, though this is rare. More commonly, the sides are fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its center fractures, it tilts downwards, causing severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And sometimes blood may be expelled, and the depression in the fractured bone becomes apparent and is not hidden to the sense. To treat it, the patient should lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulders, then press their shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides with the hands, carefully aligning them as much as possible.
Continue until the bone returns to its proper shape, then apply a bandage and pressure, and place a splint made of a light board of poplar or willow or something similar over it after wrapping it in cloth. Then carefully tie it to the fractured bone so that it does not move, wrap the bandage around in a circular manner towards the back several times and secure it tightly, then check the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity calls for it to be loosened due to suppuration, pain, or swelling in the area, then promptly loosen it, remove the bandage, and address whatever has occurred with its appropriate treatment, then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And apply tension to it until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
On the setting of ribs when broken, know that the break in the ribs occurs in the thick parts adjacent to the back, and their ends from the front are only subjected to compression because they are cartilaginous. The knowledge of this is not hidden upon examination with the fingers.
Set it by aligning the break with your fingers in a manner that the shape becomes as it should be, then bandage it and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the rib fracture is inclined inward, it will cause severe pain to the patient.
And it perforates like the perforation that occurs to one with pleurisy because the bone perforates the membrane, and shortness of breath, coughing, and spitting up a lot of blood also occur. This is difficult to treat, and predecessors have employed various methods to deal with it.
Some say that the patient's diet should include foods that produce gas to inflate the belly and expand it, pushing the fracture outward. However, we discourage this so as not to cause a hot swelling if it has not already occurred; if it has occurred, it will only exacerbate and confirm it.
Some have said that a cupping glass should be placed on the area and then suctioned strongly, which seems reasonable except for the concern that the cupping glass might pull too much from the weakened area. Others say that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
And the padding is placed between the ribs until it fills, making the bandage even when wrapped around, then treat the patient with the treatment for fractures of food and medicine, if the patient suffers a severe exhaustion that he cannot bear. And the bone was piercing the membrane painfully, and we feared for the patient, so it should be that we incise the area and expose the fractured rib, then place beneath it the device that protects the peritoneum which has been previously described, and cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it with ointments until it heals. If during this time a hot swelling appears, act quickly, soak the padding in rose oil, place it on the area, and treat the patient internally with what alleviates the swelling as well.
And he should lie on the side that allows him to sleep more comfortably until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of spinal and neck fractures.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, which is rare, and more often they suffer from contusions; the same applies to the vertebrae of the back. If this happens to someone and you want to know whether they will heal or not. Then observe, if you see that his hands have become weak, numb, and lifeless, and he is unable to move them, stretch them, or grasp with them, and when pricked or pierced with a needle, he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that he will not heal in most cases and he is doomed.
And if he moves them and feels biting and pricking in them, know that the bone marrow is safe and the patient will recover with treatment. If the vertebrae are similarly affected and you want to know if they will also recover or not.
Look at his legs, if you see that they have become weak and what happened to the hands has also occurred in them, then when he lies on his back, gas and feces are expelled involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate but is unable to do so, know that he is doomed, so do not bother with his treatment. If none of these symptoms appear, the condition is milder. The treatment for what has occurred is to aim to reduce the hot swelling.
Place on the affected vertebra rose oil alone or mixed with roasted egg yolks. Apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening, drying poultices to the area, bind it tightly with a bandage, and order him to rest and remain still.
He should only sleep on the side that does not cause him pain until he recovers. If a fragment of bone or something else has become detached due to the fracture, it is necessary to cut open the skin and remove that piece of bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, then treat it with unifying ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the tail, which is the tip, breaks, you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus.
And the broken bone is aligned with the other hand as much as possible and alignment is achieved, then apply bandaging and splinting if necessary, then secure it.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, incise over it and remove it, and treat the wound as described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of hip fracture.
The vertebrae rarely break, but if they do break, the fracture occurs at their ends where they crumble and split lengthwise, tilting inward. The patient experiences pain in the affected area, swelling, and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
To set it, place your hand over the fracture to understand its shape. If the fracture is only at the ends, align that fracture as much as possible to resemble its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach to set the fracture. Once aligned, apply bandaging and then place a splint made of wood or leather over it and secure it tightly.
Do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the shifting of the splint. Fill in the concavities of the ribs to ensure even tension. Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side. If swelling occurs, apply a warm poultice.
Stop extending and setting it until the warm swelling subsides. Apply what is necessary to alleviate it as previously described, then return to setting and securing it properly. If there are fragments or crumbling at the ends of the bone, they should not be removed.
And it is not touched but leveled from the outside as we said and its dressing is left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the Setting of Humerus Fractures.
The humerus is what lies between the elbow and the shoulder. If it fractures, it should be set in one of two ways. The first is to take a smooth, moderately thick curved stick as follows:
And ties two bands to its ends, then hangs it from a high place. The patient sits on a chair, then throws his fractured arm over the stick until his armpit is attached to the center of the curvature of the stick, then suspends something heavy above it.
Or a servant extends it downwards, then the doctor aligns the fracture with both hands until the broken parts are set in place. The other method is for the patient to lie on his back and have his hand suspended from his neck with a band.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with both hands and the other to hold below it, each pulling in their direction. If you want the extension to be stronger, tie a band below the fracture and another above it, and have each servant pull them in their direction.
If the fracture is close to the shoulder joint, it is best for the middle of the band to be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, the band should be placed at that location and on the elbow itself.
Then align the fracture gently without violence until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then bind it if there is no inflammation. If there is inflammation, leave binding until the seventh day, and apply a cloth cleansed and soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the inflammation subsides, then bind it. The method of binding is to place a dressing on the fracture, then wrap a strip of new cloth over the dressing. Gather the arm around the upper arm itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And use the cloth and binding on the upper arm and arm so that the arm serves as a splint, unless something prevents you from doing so and nothing changes in the fractured bone. If you fear that something might change due to that, then use splints.
It involves placing on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, with an interval of one finger's width between each splint. The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, adding three fingers' width from each side.
Then bind the splints with the binding method I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
It is that your binding at the site of the fracture should be tighter.
The binding should be less tight as you move away from the fracture. If you see that placing the splints and binding as we described at the time of setting the limb is appropriate, then do so.
If you fear inflammation, then leave the binding and splints until the seventh day as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days to ensure that no itching, swelling, or obstruction of nourishment to the limb occurs due to excessive tightness, and adjust accordingly as mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will occur, do not loosen the bandage except after many days, and the patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a pillow filled with wool should be placed under his armpit, moderately full.
And check at all times, whether night or day, to ensure that the shape of the broken limb does not deteriorate or the bandage loosens; correct everything with your effort. Make the patient's diet as we have mentioned, the food should be mild initially until the bone begins to strengthen.
It is necessary to make his diet more substantial, for it is usual for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days. At that time, the bandage should be removed, he should use the bath, and be treated with ointments suitable for this purpose. If the fracture is severe and infected.
Do not remove the bandage from him until fifty days or two months have passed.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of an arm fracture.
The arm is composed of two bones, known as the forearm bones. One is smaller and lies next to the thumb, and the other is larger and is placed below the smaller one. The larger forearm bone may fracture alone, or the smaller one may fracture alone, or both may fracture together.
When the upper smaller bone is fractured, setting it is easier and healing is faster. When the lower bone is fractured, the fracture is worse and healing is more difficult. The worst case is when both bones are fractured together. If the fractured bone is the upper smaller one.
The physician, when setting [the bone], should make his traction mild and gently until he aligns it. If the larger phalanx is fractured, he should make his traction stronger. If both phalanges are fractured, he should make the traction very strong indeed.
It is advisable that the shape of the hand be placed extended on a cushion when setting and traction are applied, with the thumb of the hand raised above all the fingers, and the little finger below the other fingers, and the patient sitting cross-legged.
And let the cushion be at his level in height so that the patient does not incur any difficulty.
Then, one servant stretches the arm from below, either with his hand or with a band, and another servant stretches from above in the same manner. Then, the physician aligns the bone until he restores it to the best possible form. If there are fragments in the broken bone, they should be removed.
Place each fragment in its position to the best of your ability. If a protruding fragment appears and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, incise over it and extract it as described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have dedicated a separate section for it.
Seek the treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of setting, apply a cloth dipped in a mixture made with rose oil and white wax. It should be of moderate thickness between thick and thin, and bind the cloth gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the poultice and apply a dressing prepared from mill powder mixed with egg white. Then, apply bandages, and let the bandage placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger. Know that the number of bandages for the arm is usually six.
The fracture was in one molar or in both molars together, then make your binding at the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and every time you turn the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding a little looser as mentioned earlier at the beginning of the chapter.
Let the bandages that wrap around the fracture be soft and moist, not very hard. Let the thread used for binding be of special cotton, intermediate between thinness and thickness as described, and examine the limb and ligament after a few days.
If something requires repair, such as an itch that occurs in the limb, the limb should be washed with warm water until the itch subsides, and the limb should be left unbound for a night to rest, then binding is resumed.
If the binding has loosened or the bone has moved, correct all of that to the best of your ability. Also, if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, you should loosen it slightly and leave it for a few days until the nourishment can reach it, then bind it again.
If nothing mentioned occurs to the patient, it should not be loosened until after twenty days or so. Then, suspend the patient's arm to his neck and let his arm be in a balanced position. He should avoid restless movements and sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days or thirty-two, and it may heal in twenty-eight days, all of which depends on the conditions of the humors and the strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of fractures in the hand and fingers.
The palm's keratin and the fingertips rarely suffer fractures, but they often experience contusions. Whenever a fracture or contusion occurs in the palm.
It is advisable for the patient to sit cross-legged with a chair level in front of him.
Then he should place his hand on it, outstretched. The bone servant then stretches the broken bone, and the doctor aligns it until it is properly set. At that point, bandaging and pressure should be applied unless there is a hot swelling, then a cast should be placed over it according to the area.
And it has been wrapped in a soft cloth. If the fracture is toward the palm's underside, make a ball-like shape from the cloth and have the patient grasp it with their fractured hand, then secure it with a long strip of cloth.
Let the cast be made of leather with flexibility so that it conforms to the bending of the entire palm and is secured as necessary. If the fracture is on the outside, a cast should be made from above and another from below in the palm so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then secure the binding as the hand is rotated and interlace between the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture occurs in one of the fingertips, if it is the thumb, align it properly and then secure it with the palm. If you prefer, you may make a small upright cast to support the fracture.
And if it does not move, then if the fracture is for all the fingers such as the middle finger, index finger, little finger or ring finger, they should be aligned and bound to the adjacent healthy finger, or they are all bound in a straight position which is better, or a small splint is placed on them.
As we said about the thumb and examine during and after setting it, if there is any swelling or heat, treat it accordingly as has been described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On setting the femur fracture.
The thigh bone often fractures and can be detected by sensation as it shifts forward and backward. Setting it involves placing a bandage above the fracture and another below it while the patient lies face down.
Then, each supporting bandage is stretched to its side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. If it is close to the base of the thigh, it is advisable to use a soft bandage, either of wool or something similar.
At the base of the thigh toward the groin, the stretch should be upward and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee so that the stretch is downward. Then, the doctor aligns the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural form.
The bone will heal well. At that point, the dressing and binding should be maintained if there is no hot swelling in the limb. If swelling does occur, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to its treatment.
And as for binding it, you should fold a strong and wide bandage around the fracture two or three times and leave what remains, then bind the leg until the heel reaches the base of the shin, and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, and let the ends of the thread be above on both sides.
Then wrap what remains of the bandage around the leg and thigh, then place the splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and make one splint for the shin bone, then fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft cloths so that the binding is even.
Then begin with a strong bind from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns, and the further the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the looser and gentler your binding should be.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, tie them to the splints above, then pass the ends of the thread down until they reach the heel, and also bind the other ends of the splints with them.
To ensure the binding does not shift from its place, then leave it bound as long as there is no sign of injury, swelling, or inflammation in the member. If any of these occur, immediately loosen it and correct whatever has happened as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, you should smooth it out if possible; otherwise, cut into it and remove it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be bound without adding the leg to it with splints.
As we mentioned in the arm and forearm, except that setting it this way does not cause limping for the patient. However, if you set it alone without attaching the leg to it, the person will inevitably limp forever.
And know that the thigh strengthens in fifty days, or slightly more or less, all of this depending on the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely fractures but is often subject to contusions. If it does fracture, it would either be a split or a shattering of its parts, whether with an open wound or without.
All of this can be determined by touch, and setting it involves aligning the separated parts with your fingers until they come together and unify as much as possible with care, precision, and gentleness. Then apply a dressing, and if necessary, a circular splint over it.
Tighten above with the appropriate bandage for that, then attend to all the conditions we described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, by addressing each symptom with what is suitable until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On setting the fracture of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the fibula. Both are subject to various types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, its setting is like the setting of the arm, and the procedure is the same. If both bones are broken, the leg turns in all directions; if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns forward; if the thicker bone is broken, and this happens below, then it is not hidden from you.
Use stretching and leveling, and bind the splints accordingly. However, if the break in the leg is severe with many shards, the stretching should be less and gentler, and you should be more careful in setting it.
And in the leg, there is an additional procedure compared to the arm. When you have leveled the splints and completed all your work, take two strips of fir wood which are used for leveling the gaps between the boards, or they can be made from palm leaves or similar materials.
Choose those that are slightly thick and not too thin, and let their length be the same as the leg from the knee down. Then wrap a cloth around each one twice along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let it extend from the knee to the foot. Then bind the two strips at three points, the ends and the middle. With this restraint, the leg will not tilt to the right or left and will be set properly. Sometimes a wooden gutter of the length of the leg can also be used.
It is placed there to prevent movement, and it is most necessary to do this in the case of a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially. Then examine the leg every day and pay close attention to any swelling or inflammation or other such conditions. And when any of that occurs, counter it with what is appropriate until it heals, and the bone of the leg will heal in thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fractures of the leg and fingers.
As for the heel, it is not subject to fracture at all. As for the bones of the leg, they may be subject to fracture, and fingers rarely suffer fractures; rather, they are more often subjected to contusions in most cases.
