If the leg bones are fractured and you see that those bones are approaching each other, let the patient stand with his foot on the ground as if walking. Then you place your foot on the raised part of those bones and press them down to align them.
Until they return to their places, then apply the dressing and splint. Place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, then secure the foot tightly after wrapping it with bandages and anything else needed.
If three or four days have passed, loosen the binding. You will find the bones aligned whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as you see. As for if some of the fingers are broken, set and align them according to what has been described to you for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along its length, but slightly wider than the finger. Then place under the foot the board I have described to you and secure it tightly. If more than one bone, two or three or more, has been fractured.
And make on each finger a splint of appropriate size wrapped in a soft cloth and bind the board at the bottom of the foot and let each end of the board protrude from the sole of the foot to hold it firmly in place.
You should not forget to attend to all that we have mentioned to you regarding the symptoms of fractures, and address each symptom as appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When the woman's vagina is fractured, seat her in a squatting position, then bend her slightly towards her back little by little. Let her hold on from behind. Then the midwife should stuff her vagina with cotton until it fills and becomes like a ball inside her vagina.
Then the woman should be shaken and her torso raised little by little. The cotton will come out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina, and the bone fracture will return to its place. Then a pad should be placed on her back. Whenever she wishes to urinate, gently remove the cotton so that she may urinate.
Return it in the same manner as it was first inserted, then go back to her initial padding. Do this for seven days or so, and it will heal. If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, attach a reed tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, and the fracture will return to its place. Then fill it with cotton and keep it in place as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it should be treated similarly.
And its adjustment is as we described in the hip bone, and it will not be hidden from you the correctness in these rare fractures that seldom occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself guides you in most cases.
On the right way in setting and binding it, understand. As for when a man's thigh is fractured like a duck's throat, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth on it and bind it, leaving it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the setting of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is necessary for one who has sustained a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the thigh or arm bone or the like, to promptly perform bloodletting at that time if the conditions for bloodletting assist you, as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding, you should promptly cauterize it by applying powdered zinc if nothing else is available. Then proceed to set the fracture on that very day and do not delay it.
If a hot swelling does not occur, then set the fracture immediately. If a hot swelling does occur, leave setting the fracture until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides. Do not touch it at all on the third and fourth days, for doing so would causeä¸è‰¯ååº”ã€‚è¯·æ³¨æ„ï¼Œæˆ‘å°½åŠ›æä¾›å‡†ç¡®çš„ç¿»è¯‘ï¼Œä½†æ¶‰åŠå¤æ–‡æˆ–ä¸“ä¸šæœ¯è¯­æ—¶å¯èƒ½å­˜åœ¨ç†è§£åå·®ã€‚è¿™é‡Œç›´æŽ¥ç¿»è¯‘å¯èƒ½ä¼šåŒ…å«ä¸€äº›åŒ»å­¦ä¸Šä¸å¤ªå‡†ç¡®çš„è¡¨è¾¾ï¼Œ"ä¸è‰¯ååº”"åœ¨åŽŸæ–‡ä¸­çš„æ„æ€æ˜¯å¦‚æžœåœ¨è¿™ä¸ªæ—¶é—´æ®µå¤„ç†ä¼¤å£ä¼šå¯¼è‡´ä¸å¥½çš„åŽæžœã€‚
If the broken bone protrudes and is exposed on the skin, you should gently aim to reduce and align it with your hands using slight traction. If you cannot reduce and align it with your hands, use this tool, which is made of iron and is about seven or eight fingers in length. And expose it according to the size of the wound.
For this reason, the doctor should take three or four of them according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture. They should be rounded with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed upon during use.
It should be sharp at the end, with a curve at its tip. The top should be thicker and from the middle down much thinner. This is its shape:.
It is called "peron" in Greek, meaning a small lever. The sharp and curved end should be placed on the protruding bone and pushed once until the bone returns and straightens somewhat, thus beginning to align the broken ends with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, you should cut that end of the bone until the instrument can grip it. If you are unable to realign the bone as described, then cut it using a method similar to those we have mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws however you can, then remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If after realigning the bone the patient experiences severe painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to realign it to its natural position, do so, for you greatly benefit the patient. Once you have completed setting the bone, soak a cloth in a constricting black liquid, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any fat or ointment to the wound.
To prevent putrefaction and corruption from occurring, then use bandages during your time off from setting the bone and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole with scissors in the bandage according to the size of the wound.
Beware exceedingly lest you bind the wound with the fracture, for ignorant physicians have often done this and caused either death or gangrene or pneumonia in their patients. Let your binding be soft and loose, contrary to the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is poor or it is a large wound and you fear some of theä¸è‰¯ç—‡çŠ¶æˆ‘ä»¬æè¿°è¿‡çš„ï¼Œä»–åœ¨è¯¥éƒ¨ä½æ„Ÿåˆ°å›°æ‰°çš„ç–¼ç—›ï¼Œä½ ä¸åº”è¯¥å°†å¤¹æ¿æ”¾åœ¨ä¸Šé¢ã€‚ç”¨åšå›ºçš„å¸ƒæ–™åˆ¶æˆç»·å¸¦æ›¿ä»£å¤¹æ¿ï¼Œå¹¶ç”¨å…¶å›ºå®šã€‚
If it has been a day or two and you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that was placed on it with the drink, then use bandages and ointments which are our custom to treat wounds with, such as the four-ingredient ointment and the like, and you should loosen the binding.
You should examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it heals. You should position the limb in such a way that pus can easily drain downward. If days pass and the wound does not close and the pus does not cease, know that there are likely small bone splinters present.
You should probe the wound with aæŽ¢é’ˆ. If any of those splinters are loose, remove and extract them. For those that are not loose and cause pain by irritating the limb, cut and remove them in whatever way you can manage.
If the wound is exposed to cold, bites, or any other form of corruption and putrefaction, you should counter each condition with a corresponding treatment as previously described in its respective section.
Among the things you should pay attention to and keep in mind when a large bone, such as the thigh or the forearm, or other similar major limbs, is broken, do not attempt to pull it or extract it. Often, doing so can lead to death. Therefore, leave it until it putrefies; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then you should treat the wound if you see an opportunity for treatment; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the swelling that occurs after some fractures.
This swelling often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially near the joints, which can deform the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from performing its natural functions.
If the swelling is soft, use medicines that constrict, such as frankincense, mastic, aloes, squill, and hellebore, or similar. Take some or all of these, and mix them with a constricting potion or with egg white or vinegar.
Apply it to the swelling and bind it tightly, leaving it bound for many days. Then remove the binding and repeat with another if necessary until the swelling goes away, God willing, or secure a sheet of lead over it tightly.
For lead has a property that can reduce any excess in the limbs. If the swelling has hardened and become severe, and necessity requires its removal, cut into it from above and remove the excess or pare it down with a sharp instrument until it is gone, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On treating fractures when they have healed and the limb remains weak, unlike its original nature.
If a bone fracture has healed and the limb remains weak afterward, this can be due to many reasons. One of them is either due to excessive loosening and tying of the bandages improperly, or due to overly tightening the bandages so that nourishment cannot reach the limb, or due to excessive and improper interference, or due to excessive movements at inappropriate times, or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment for that is nourishing the patient and fattening his body until blood increases in it, using the bath, bringing joy and happiness to him, and so on. Then apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch attracts a lot of nourishment or continue to moisten it with warm water. Until the nourishment flows in it and returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of fractured bones that have healed crookedly and prevented their proper function.
If a limb that has been fractured and healed exhibits deformity, protrusion of the broken bone, or complexity, causing the limb to appear ugly. Unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, it should not be accepted that one would claim to break the limb again. Many ignorant doctors and bone setters in our country have done this.
This action is highly condemnable and leads to great harm, the least of which is disability. However, if the deformity and complexity are pliable, it should be treated with water in which relaxing herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves and roots of the sycamore and marjoram, and the like.
And it is dressed with soft bandages like well-made diachylon, or the juice of the root of the colocynth is taken and mixed with chicken fat and ceratum, then used for dressing, or take the juicy figs and pound them with dove droppings and similar medications which are called dispersers of adhesions.
And the complexity is resolved by continuous gentle massage with the hands and moving the limb in all directions at all times. If the deformity has persisted, intensified, and become rigid, and it becomes necessary to treat it with iron.
It is necessary to incise above it, release the bone connection, and cut any remaining complexity or bone with delicate cuts, applying gentleness in this process with effort and care, then treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its position, which hinders movement, uglifies the appearance of the limb, and causes severe pain to the patient. Whenever someone suffers from jaw dislocation, it should be promptly reduced at once.
It should not be delayed at all, for if it is delayed, the area will swell and make reducing the jaw difficult. Therefore, it should not be postponed, nor moved or stretched while swollen, as this often causes spasms and painful discomfort to the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should immediately begin treating the patient. Then, it is left until the swelling subsides slightly, after which the limb is treated with hot water and oil, then gently reduced. Each part is treated as described in its respective section.
And I have arranged the chapters on dislocation in accordance with what was previously mentioned regarding fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the Treatment of the Lower Jaw Dislocation.
Dislocations of the jawbones rarely occur except in cases of trauma, and their dislocation can happen in two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their positions and become somewhat lax, or they are completely and entirely dislocated until they become lax. Until it approaches the chest, causing the saliva of the patient to flow and unable to hold it back, nor can they close their jaw, and their tongue stutters when speaking. If the dislocation is minor, it often returns on its own with minimal intervention.
And if the dislocation is complete, it should be corrected immediately without delay. This is done by having an attendant hold the patient's head while the doctor inserts the thumb of one hand at the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side.
Or if the dislocation is on both sides, he should insert both thumbs inside the mouth and use the rest of his fingers outside to align them. The doctor orders the patient to relax their jaw and let it go in any direction while the doctor realigns the jaw and pushes it back to its proper position.
If it is difficult to realign it, especially if both jawbones are dislocated, then use a compress with hot water and oil until they can be easily realigned. Do not delay their realignment as previously mentioned. Once they are returned to their proper position and the patient's mouth closes without laxity, then apply bandages reinforced with strips made from wax, oil, and roses.
Then it is tied gently with a loose bandage, and the patient should sleep on his back with his head elevated between two pillows so that he does not move to the right or left nor chew anything but rather make his food liquid and soft.
Until the pain disappears and the jaw heals, let him eat what he desires, but he should do so gently without forcing his mouth open while eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw sets and heals.
If the jaws do not close properly at some point and do not return to their places, fever and persistent headache often occur, and the patient may suffer from diarrhea or vomit bile. If you see this, know that it is a sign of corruption.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the Reduction of the Clavicle and Shoulder Joint.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest, but it may dislocate outward, which can be determined by touch. To reduce it, the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the area with your palm firmly, and it will return to place. Then apply a dressing and bandage it tightly. As for the end that connects to the shoulder, it rarely dislocates. If it does dislocate one day, it should be reduced and aligned as mentioned, and in any way that seems appropriate to you.
Then apply the dressing, bandages, and binding, and instruct the patient to remain rested and still until healed. With this exact treatment, you also realign a dislocated shoulder joint.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the realignment of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint may dislocate in three ways: one is when it dislocates towards the armpit downwards, the second is when it dislocates towards the chest, and sometimes it dislocates upwards from the shoulder, which is rare.
It does not dislocate backwards due to the position of the shoulder, nor does it dislocate forwards due to the presence of the nerves. It most often dislocates and moves downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have less flesh, as it quickly exits and enters in such individuals.
As for those who have a lot of flesh, it is the opposite; I mean, it exits and enters with difficulty. Sometimes some people may receive a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and become warm, leading them to assume that it has dislocated. It should be examined to determine if this is true.
Then proceed with its treatment. The dislocation towards the armpit downwards is identified if you align the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one; you will find a noticeable difference between them, and you will feel that the head of the dislocated shoulder has sunk in, and beneath the armpit, upon touching, the head of the shoulder feels like an egg.
The patient cannot raise his hand to his ear or move it in all its usual motions. Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will clearly feel this upon touch, and it cannot be hidden.
And this jaw can be easily replaced if it is soft or the patient is a child. The replacement involves raising the servant's hand up, then placing your thumbs under his armpit and lifting the joint forcefully upwards to its place while the servant raises his hand and stretches it upwards.
Then, it is placed downwards as it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned and the jaw has been like this for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use laxatives that relax and soften, such as cooking the root of the carrot and fenugreek and marigold in water and using it.
Then, the patient lies on his back and a ball of wool, which is moderate between softness and firmness, is placed under his armpit. Then the doctor places his knee on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and stretches it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, then it returns to its place. If you wish, you can replace it this way: have a taller person present, position them on the side of the ribs, and have their shoulder enter under the patient's armpit, lifting it upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards toward his abdomen. If the patient is light, it is advisable to hang something else from him to weigh him down, for then the jaw will return immediately. It can also be replaced in another way.
It involves fixing a long stick in the ground, with its head being round like the pestle of a mortar, neither too thick nor too thin. Then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft cloth on top of the stick, and the patient stands along the length of the stick.
Then he extends his hand below from the other side and also extends his body from the other side with force, then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it by all that we mentioned, use this treatment: take a stick about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers and its thickness is about two fingers, it should have a rounded head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:.
Then tie a soft cloth around the rounded head to prevent the stick from harming the patient, then place the stick under the armpit and extend the entire hand and arm down on the stick, and secure the stick to the upper arm, forearm, and end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a bar. Grip the width and extend the hand downward, leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side, then the joint will enter immediately. Once it enters in any possible way, it should be placed under the armpit a moderately sized ball of wool.
Then apply the prepared dressing of grindstone dust mixed with gum and egg white all over the shoulder, circling from above, then tightly secure the ball under the armpit and wrap it firmly with a bandage over the dressing, and suspend his hand to his neck.
It should be left unmoved for seven days, and the patient's diet should be light to strengthen the limb, which will speed up its healing. After seven or five days, it is released and trained through movement; if it remains stable and does not weaken, then it has healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture exposure or another ailment, it is appropriate to use cauterization with three white-hot cauteries as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If you do all of this and loosen the ligament after seven days and the joint still does not stabilize.
And if you repeatedly applied the bandage and tightened it, but it did not stabilize and fell loose, and you were unable to raise it upwards, know that the nerve at the top of the shoulder has been severed or stretched or relaxed. Know then that the joint will never be stable in its place.
As for the jaw that is displaced towards the chest and breast and behind, its reduction is done by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to place. All binding and treatment should be used until it heals. If swelling occurs in the area and movement is delayed after healing.
Let the patient use the bath frequently until that swelling softens and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of the elbow joint.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty. It can dislocate in all directions, especially forwards or backwards. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident both to sight and touch, regardless of the form of the dislocation.
When you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, it will be clearly apparent due to the deformity of the joint. The arm cannot be bent, nor can it touch the shoulder. You should promptly reduce the dislocation immediately before swelling occurs.
For if a hot swelling occurs, it is difficult to reduce and sometimes it does not heal at all, especially if the jaw is set back, for this is the worst kind of all types of jaws and the most painful, and death often comes with it.
If it can be reduced, then a servant should extend his arm with both hands and stretch out his arm, while the doctor places his hands above and below the elbow, pushing the joint back into place with the thumbs of both hands or the base of his palm until it returns to its position.
And if the jaw is forward, it can be reduced by bending the arm until the base of the palm strikes the corresponding elbow. If the jaw does not respond to being brought back, then use very strong extension, which involves two servants extending the arm.
Two servants also hold the patient to prevent any movement during the extension. Then, rotate the arm in all directions after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around his hand. When the doctor handles the joint with both hands, he should smear it with oil so that the joint can slide easily.
Then, push the joint forcefully until it returns. After it is returned, it should be bandaged with something that constricts and dries, including egg white, and tightly secured. The arm should be suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then, if the joint remains in place, remove the binding. If you see that the joint has not become firm enough, reapply the bandage and binding for several days until it becomes firm, then remove it. If swelling occurs after the joint is returned to its place and the bindings are removed.
Difficulty in movement, then use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and squeezing until it becomes soft or place on the joint a thick sheepskin pad then bind it and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and enter him into the bath. If he sweats, then rub the joint moderately.
Then apply the pad again, a second and third time, with entering the bath until it becomes soft. If you want to put moistened, light ox bile with fat on it and bind it tightly, do this several times, for it will become soft and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Wrist Dislocation.
The wrist of the hand often dislocates and reducing its dislocation is easier compared to other joints. However, it is necessary to quickly reduce its dislocation at the moment it occurs before the area swells or a hot swelling develops.
Reducing the dislocation involves placing the patient's wrist on a board, having an assistant stretch the hand, and the doctor placing his palm on the protruding joint and pushing it until it returns. However, it is necessary to check if the dislocation has moved to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the palm of his hand on the board during extension and flexion. If the dislocation is protruding to the outer side of the hand, then let the inner part of his hand be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls on the same joint protrusion. If it returns immediately, otherwise bind it with a bandage to reduce swelling.
Leave it alone and do not repeat it, for it cannot withstand repetition, and you will not be able to reset it after a few days have passed, except that the joint remains out of place, and it does not harm the patient anything except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been cut or damaged, and there is no remedy for it except to sew it up, which may be helpful or not. Once the wrist is reset, apply the dressing we described, then bind it and leave it for five days.
Then it is loosened and the hand is exercised. If moving it is difficult and some hardness appears, soften it with warm water and rubbing repeatedly until it becomes flexible.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Treatment of Separating the Fingers.
Fingers may separate in any direction. When a finger separates to the outer or inner side of the palm, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then bandage the tip of the finger and suspend it toward the direction it separated from.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight on that day. If it is at night, bind it as described. Continue to release it during the day and exercise it by movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it separates to the inner side of the hand, binding it toward the same direction and proceeding as you did initially until it heals. Do this similarly whenever it separates in any other directions.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of spinal tuberculosis.
When one of the vertebrae of the spine or neck has completely dislocated or many have moved from their places, there is no treatment for it because death quickly comes to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excrement comes out without will, unable to hold it back, and often some of his limbs weaken, either his legs or his arms or one of them. As for if a single vertebra moves from its place, it often returns to its position.
Its displacement can occur in four directions. That which moves backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should assess; if the hump has occurred since childhood, there is no treatment for it and it will never heal. As for those that have occurred due to a fall or blow or the like.
The earlier scholars have mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy discourse, most of which does not prove beneficial. I have condensed that to what suffices, so that a little of this may replace much of what they provided, by my approximation of the meaning and explanation thereof. I have also illustrated the apparatus differently from how they depicted and explained it.
I say that the hump which occurs in front on the chest cannot be treated and will not heal, and likewise those on the sides. Only the one that occurs specifically on the back is treated, as I am about to describe, which is to lay the patient face down.
On a flat bench near a wall, place a moist cloth under him to prevent his chest from being harmed. Then place a vertical wooden board planted in a hole in the ground at one end of the bench toward his head, and another wooden board toward his feet at the other end of the bench.
A servant holds the board, and let it not be fixed in the hole, and another servant holds the other [board] in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the board at his head and tie it there.
Then bind with another bandage over his hips, above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the ties and secure them to the other board at his feet. Then each servant pulls the board in his direction with the tie, and the two boards do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not fixed as we said, and the doctor places his hands on the abscess with force until it bursts, or he puts a board on it then leans on the board with his feet until it bursts. If it does not burst with this treatment, take a board that is about three arms' length in length.
And dig in the wall which we said should be near the patient a place where one end of the board can fit, then place the center of the board on the swelling and have the doctor put his feet on the other end and pull tightly until it presses on the abscess.
And it returns to its place. If you wish to make it with a hand-turned screw, then drive into the ground near the patient's head at the end of the bench two boards that are an arm's length long, and similarly at his feet at the other end of the bench two boards.
And let there be a span between each board, and make a hole in each board through which the screw can pass. Secure all the boards firmly to the ground so that they do not move at all, and insert a rounded rod, which is the screw where the rope is twisted, into the holes of the two boards.
And at its end, there is a hole in which a stick of one span length is firmly tied with what is twisted, and the same for the other two boards. Then the rope that was tightened on the patient's chest is attached to the spool at his head, and the ropes that were tightened on his legs are attached to the spool at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each spool, twisting his hand with the twister that twists the spool, and the doctor adjusts the hump as previously described. This is an illustration of the spool, the bench, and the patient.
Then after the vertebra returns and the area is even, it is appropriate to carry the dried dressing with egg white, then apply pressure. Then place a splint of a board on top of the dressing that is three fingers wide or so, and its length should be enough to cover the area of the hump.
And on some of the correct beads, then it should be tied with an appropriate bandage. The patient should consume a mild diet until healed. If there remains some protrusion in the area at the end of healing, it is appropriate to use medications that relax and soften, along with using the board we described for a long time.
A sheet of lead may be used for that. Sometimes a protrusion appears at the end of the spinal beads, which might be mistaken for dislocation; it could be an extra bone that has protruded. Do not treat this condition with such therapy, as death might occur.
The Thirty-First Chapter.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation and do not suffer from the slight displacement andå‡¹é™· that other joints do, and the hip joint can be dislocated in four ways.
This is because it can dislocate inward, outward, forward, and backward. It most often dislocates inward and rarely dislocates forward or backward. The sign of its dislocation inward is that if you align the healthy leg with the affected one, the healthy leg will appear longer.
The knee will protrude more than normal, and the affected individual will not be able to bend their leg at the joint. The area adjacent to the joint will showæ˜Žæ˜¾çš„è‚¿èƒ€ï¼Œå› ä¸ºè‚¡éª¨çš„å¤´éƒ¨å·²ç»ç§»ä½åˆ°é‚£é‡Œäº†ã€‚
The signs of outward dislocation are the opposite of these symptoms. For forward dislocation, he will fully extend his leg but will not be able to bend it without knee pain. If he attempts to walk, he will not be able to move forward.
He will retain urine, his ankle will droop, and when walking, he will step on his heel. For backward dislocation, he will not be able to fully extend the knee or bend it before bending the ankle, and his leg will also be shorter than the other.
The ankle will be relaxed, and the head of the femur will beæ˜Žæ˜¾åœ¨è…°éƒ¨ä½ç½®ã€‚è‡³äºŽè¿™ç§è„±ä½çš„å¤ä½ï¼Œå¦‚æžœä½ å‘çŽ°è„±ä½å·²ä¹…ï¼Œå·²ç»ä¸Žæ‚£è€…åŒåœ¨ï¼Œä¸”æœªå°è¯•å¤ä½å¹¶ä¿æŒåŽŸçŠ¶ï¼Œåˆ™å®Œå…¨æ— æ³•æ²»ç–—ã€‚
You should not expose it, and as for the one who had his jaw dislocated recently, whether it is any of the four types of jaw dislocation, you should immediately twist the joint and extend it inward and outward and move it right and left, perhaps.
If it returns without needing further treatment, then if it does not return this way, you should prepare a servant as an aid who is strong to extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee on his leg, and another servant to pull him from above.
He should insert his hands under his armpits, then tighten a soft bandage at the root of the thigh, and another servant should hold the end of the bandage, extending either from the front, from the side of the ribs to the side of the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
Their extension should all be done at once until the patient is lifted off the ground and remains suspended. This type of extension is common to the four types. If the jaw returns as we said, otherwise each type requires the specific treatment I will mention.
As for its specific reduction if the jaw is displaced inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, then a bandage should be placed at the root of the thigh between the hip joint and the area below the ribs.
Then extend the bandage upward from the side of the ribs to the upper body towards the clavicle. Then another strong servant should take hold with his arms and embrace the thick part of the patient's thigh and pull outward with a strong pull, for it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment used to replace this organ. If it becomes difficult for you and does not respond at all to this type of treatment, you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong flexible bandage around the ankles and knees.
There should be the width of four fingers between each one and its neighbor, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by the width of two fingers. Then, suspend the patient from a beam in the house with his head up, and there should be an arm's length between him and the ground.
Then, you order a strong boy to hold the thigh and another boy to grasp the patient. The other boy holding the thigh should push with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly. As for when it is dislocated outward, its reduction is similar.
The patient should lie on the table as we described for the hunchback case, and the bandage should be tied specifically to his affected leg and chest. Then, place two beams, one at his feet and the other at his head. Place an additional beam in the middle of the table, secured very tightly.
It should be wrapped with wet cloths to prevent injury to the patient, so that the beam is between his thighs to prevent it from being pulled down during extension. Then, each servant pulls in his direction and the doctor aligns the hip with his hands, if it responds to being returned.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it as we described for the hunchback case, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. If the dislocation is forward, you should extend the patient's leg once while he is lying in this manner on the table.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the diseased hip, then presses it with the other hand; and in this way he extends the pressure downward toward the knee. And if the dislocation is backward, it is not appropriate to extend the patient downward.
It should be raised from the ground; it is preferable that it be placed on something solid. It should also be, if his hip has dislocated outward, as we mentioned, lying on a bench on his stomach with the bandages tightly secured.
As we said before, pressure should also be applied with a board to the place where the joint has dislocated. Once the hip joint is properly returned, its return will not be hidden from you, and you will be able to extend the patient's legs.
If you see them level and the patient can bend and extend the leg without difficulty, then know that the part has returned as it should. At this point, join the thighs, apply the dressing, and bind with a turban tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then untie the bandages and remove the dressing. Compare the leg to the other one; if you see them equal in length, know that the joint has healed, and allow the patient to walk. If you notice any laxity, however, further treatment is needed.
Return, dress it, and bind it as you did initially. Leave it for another three days, then untie it. Allow him to walk slowly on it for several days until it strengthens well, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the treatment of dislocated knees.
The knee dislocates in three directions: inward, outward, and downward, which means backward. It never dislocates forward at all. The sign of its dislocation is when you ask the patient to draw his leg close to his thigh.
If it does not attach to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. The treatment for all forms of its dislocation is to have the patient sit with his legs stretched out if he can do so. Have a servant sit behind him to hold his waist and bend it slightly backward, then you sit on his thighs.
Lean your back against his front, place his leg between yours, then hold his knee with both of your palms and clasp them together with your fingers around his knee. Then, grip the sides of his knee firmly with both hands, and have another servant stretch his leg until the knee returns to its proper place.
The sign that it has returned is when the leg attaches to the thigh smoothly and without resistance. Then, dress the area and keep the leg attached to the thigh, then bandage them together for three or four days, after which you release them. Only light walking should be done for a few days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it as described, then use strong stretching with the bandages that I have previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of dislocated ankle.
The ankle may be slightly dislocated or completely dislocated, and its dislocation can either be inward or outward. The sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has dislocated.
As for the treatment of slight dislocation, it is easy to reduce it by gently stretching with both hands and aligning until it returns. As for the treatment if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit down and a strong servant should hold him from behind in the middle of his back.
Then, hold his foot with your right hand from the top and with your left hand below the foot at the ankle. Then, pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand twice, then stretch it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as described, then on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts and you see the foot is aligned, otherwise repeat the procedure as it will return to place.
If it does not return as described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground and drive a peg into the ground very securely between his thighs, wrapped in cloth so as not to harm the patient. Then have an assistant hold his thigh firmly.
Then, another servant stretches the leg either with his hands or by a strap tied around the ankle. Each servant then pulls in opposition to the other, while the peg remains between the patient's thighs to prevent the body from being pulled downward during stretching. The doctor then aligns the dislocation with his hands.
Another servant holds the healthy leg downwards, then the dislocation returns quickly. When the dislocation has returned and you are sure of its correct return, take the dressing and bandage it tightly with strong bindings and splint the foot downward. You should be careful of the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
So that the binding is not too tight and does not harm him, then leave it for two or three days. If the binding becomes loose, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should avoid walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, you cannot be assured that the joint will not dislocate again and become irreparable, making treatment ineffective afterward. If a hot swelling appears, you should use what we have previously described in another section of the treatment and continue until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
It should be aligned gently without force where it has become dislocated, which is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the toes on the top of the foot, the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
or on a board while standing as if walking. Then you place your foot on top of the dislocated joint and press it firmly with your foot until it returns to its proper position and you see that it is aligned without any protrusion in the area.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that covers the entire foot, which has two ends, and bind it tightly for three days. Then untie it and keep him from walking for many days until it becomes strong and you are assured it will not recur, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of jaw that may occur with a wound or with a fracture or both together.
When any of this occurs and you treat it and set it, it is often followed by death.
Therefore, one should not undertake the treatment of such conditions unless they are skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, patient, not reckless, and not overly bold. Initially, they should use medicines that alleviate hot swellings only and leave the patient untouched otherwise.
Except, O Lord, for what I hope will be safe from damage with a mild illness and where some hope is apparent to you, then do not delay treating it at the very beginning before a hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns as desired, then use treatments that alleviate hot swellings and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments. If the jaw is fractured and there are detached bone splinters, do not delay in extracting them. Follow in this what we have mentioned regarding simple diseases as presented earlier in their respective sections.
Exert your utmost effort and abstain from taking risky paths as my previous advice to you has indicated. That will preserve your reputation and safeguard your honor, if God wills.
